Preface
This Master’s thesis is submitted as a partial fulﬁlment of the requirements for the degree
of Master of Science in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The
project is credited to 30 ECTS points for each author, for a total of 60 ECTS points. The
topic of the project was suggested by the supervisors, however, small adjustments have
been made during the process in collaboration between the authors and the supervisors.
The thesis is a part of the work done in IEA-EBC Annex 68 and is built upon the Master’s
thesis Control strategies for mechanical ventilation in low-energy residences by Ambra
Guglietti and Martina Pesavento, June 2017. This Master’s thesis was written in cooper-
ation with the Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark
under the supervision of Jakub Kolarik and Daria Zukowska-Tejsen.
We would like to thank Jakub Kolarik and Daria Zukowska-Tejsen for the supervi-
sion, guidance and assistance throughout the project. We would also like to thank Rune
Korsholm Andersen for his guidance and sharing of numerous publications used in this
project.
Kgs. Lyngby June 21, 2018

i
==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
Abstract
This Master’s thesis is part of IEA-EBC Annex 68 - Indoor Air Quality Design and Con-
trol in Low-energy Residential Buildings. The thesis investigated the performance of ten
different ventilation control strategies for mechanical ventilation in low-energy residen-
cies. Nine of the control strategies were demand controlled and one used constant air
volume. The demand controlled ventilation systems used either temperature, CO2, rel-
ative humidity sensor or all three sensor types in combination. The sensors were either
placed in the exhaust duct or in speciﬁc rooms of the apartment. The control strategies
were evaluated on how they affected the primary energy consumption of the residence
and how good thermal comfort, air quality and relative humidity levels they could keep in
the residence. The case used in the study is a low-energy apartment of 93.8 m2 placed in
Nordhavn, Copenhagen. It was constructed to meet the Danish building regulations and
the ventilation systems were made in accordance with the Danish building class 2020. Ten
building-related design parameters were used in the sensitivity analysis and by thorough
research in scientiﬁc literature the probability density function of these design parameters
was made and used in the sensitivity analysis.
The ventilation control strategy capable of providing the best indoor climate was a de-
mand controlled system with CO2 and temperature sensors in the bedrooms, relative hu-
midity sensors in the bathrooms and all three sensor types in the combined living room
and kitchen. A similar control strategy supplying a minimum ventilation rate of 0.1 l/s
per m2 heated ﬂoor area was investigated even though it does not comply with the cur-
rent Danish building regulation that requires a minimum ventilation rate of 0.3 l/s per m2
heated ﬂoor area. The system was able to provide the same indoor climate and robust-
ness against parameter variation in the sensitivity analysis but used 8% less energy than
the one with a higher minimum ventilation rate. The control strategy using constant air
volume performed better than some of the other more advanced control strategies.
iii
==PAGE:10==
The sensitivity analysis revealed that window-opening is a very important design pa-
rameter that has a large inﬂuence on both primary energy consumption, thermal comfort,
air quality and relative humidity levels. Setpoint for space heating is a very important
parameter for the primary energy consumption of the investigated residence.
iv
==PAGE:11==
Resumé
Dette kandidatspeciale er en del af IEA-EBC Annex 68 - Indoor Air Quality Design and
Control in Low Energy Residential Buildings. Specialet undersøgte igennem en sen-
sitivitetsanalyse ti forskellige mekaniske ventilationskontrolstrategier, hvoraf de ni var
behovsstyrede ventilationssystemer, og den sidste havde konstant luft tilførsel. De be-
hovstyrede strategier brugte enten temperatursensor, CO2 sensor, relativ luftfugtighedssen-
sor eller en kombination af de tre sensorer, som enten var placeret centralt i returkanalen
eller i speciﬁkke rum. Kontrolstrategierne blev evalueret på deres indﬂydelse på hus-
standens primære energiforbrug og deres evne til at opretholde god termisk komfort,
luftkvalitet samt luftfugtighed. Husstanden, som undersøgelsen tog udgangspunkt i, var
en ﬁreværelses lavenergi lejlighed på 93,8 m2 placeret i Nordhavn, København. Ventila-
tionssystemerne blev opbygget i henhold til den danske bygningsklasse 2020. Der blev
udvalgt ti bygningsspeciﬁkke parametre til sensitivitetsanalysen, og efter grundig litter-
atursøgning blev en sandsynlighedstæthedsfunktion af disse parametre opsat.
Resultaterne viser, at ventilationssystemet, som kan levere det bedste indeklima, er et
behovsstyret system med CO2 og temperatursensorer i soveværelserne, relativ fugtighedssen-
sorer i badeværelserne og alle tre typer sensorer i køkken-alrummet. En identisk kon-
trolstrategi med reduceret ventilationsmængde på 0,1 l/s/m2 opvarmet gulvareal blev un-
dersøgt, på trods af at det danske bygningsreglement kræver en minimumsventilations-
mængde på 0,3 l/s/m2 opvarmet gulvareal. Dette system er i stand til at levere det samme
indeklima og robusthed mod parametervariation, men med 8% lavere energiforbrug end
systemet uden reduceret ventilationsmængde. Kontrolstrategien med konstant lufttilførsel
klarer sig bedre end nogle af de mere avancerede kontrolstrategier med central sensor.
Sensitivitetsanalysen viser, at åbning af vinduer er en meget vigtig parameter med stor
effekt på både det primære energiforbrug, den termiske komfort, luftkvalitet og den rela-
tive luftfugtighed. Set-punktet for rumopvarmning viser sig også at have stor indﬂydelse
på det primære energiforbrug i den undersøgte lejlighed.
v

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
This Master’s thesis is part of IEA-EBC Annex 68 - Indoor Air Quality Design and Con-
trol in Low-energy Residential Buildings. The objectives of IEA-EBC Annex 68 is "to
provide a scientiﬁc basis for the design and operational strategies of buildings that have
minimal energy consumption, and at the same time maintain very high standards regard-
ing indoor environmental quality based on the control of sources, sinks and ﬂows of heat,
air, moisture, and pollutants under in-use conditions" [1]. IEA-EBC Annex 68 consists of
5 subtasks and this thesis worked on Subtask 4. Subtask 4 combines results from previous
subtasks of IEA-EBC Annex 68 and state of the art knowledge to bring forth the design
to accomplish optimal indoor air quality (IAQ) including optimal as well as practical op-
eration strategies in low-energy residencies. The aim is to deliver a guide on ventilation
control strategies for residencies to obtain the lowest energy consumption while providing
a good IAQ. The guide is aimed for designers, building owners and operators to support
them in identifying the best strategy for an optimal IAQ in their buildings [1].
Worldwide there has been registered an increase in energy demand and the building
sector alone is responsible for up to 40% of the energy use in the EU [2]. With the
growing understanding of global warming and its effect on the environment [3] combined
with the human cost related to air pollution from the major energy sources used today,
the need for energy saving initiatives are urgent. Heating, ventilation and air conditioning
(HVAC) systems make up a signiﬁcant part of the building’s energy demand [4]. The
Energy Performance of Building Directive (EPBD) constituted by 28 EU members and
Norway require new buildings to be nearly-zero energy buildings by 2020 [2] and energy
efﬁcient HVAC systems are key in reducing building energy demand.
In the 1970s it was ﬁrst noticed that symptoms related to poor IAQ was reported by
tenants in newly constructed buildings [5]. The symptoms were respiratory difﬁculties,
headache, fatigue and skin irritation, the medical condition is called Sick Building Syn-
drome (SBS) . Energy optimisation of buildings make buildings more air-tight which sets
1
==PAGE:26==
1. INTRODUCTION
higher demands for HVAC systems to ensure a good IAQ and thus avoid SBS.
As part of IEA-EBC Annex 68 this project tries to support the development of energy
efﬁcient ventilation systems capable of providing high level of IAQ. This is important for
the development of sustainable and healthy buildings.
2
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2
Thesis outline
An overview and a short description of the chapters in the thesis is presented here.
Chapter 3 describes the background of the project. The current Danish building regu-
lations are described. Guidelines and market preferences for ventilation control strategies
are described. State of the art within the research ﬁeld of demand controlled ventilation
in residencies as well as research using sensitivity analysis in connection with residencies
are covered.
Chapter 4 describes the residence and the ventilation control strategies to be investi-
gated and how these are to be evaluated. The design parameters chosen for the sensitivity
analysis are presented and the motivation for choosing these design parameters are ex-
plained. The basis for the data supporting the range and distribution of the design param-
eters are explained in detail. Finally the method for the sensitivity analysis is explained
in detail.
Chapter 5 shows the results of the different investigations. The ten ventilation con-
trol strategies are compared on primary energy consumption, CO2 concentration, relative
humidity and temperature.
Chapter 6 discuses and explains the results and possible explanations for why the
results are the way they are. Sources of uncertainty and errors are presented.
Chapter 7 contains the ﬁnal conclusion of the study.
Chapter 8 propose suggestions to further work.

==PAGE:28==

CHAPTER 3
Background
3.1
Requirements for residential ventilation in Denmark
The Danish building regulations [6] continually make the requirements for building venti-
lation and energy consumption stricter. Ventilation methods and ventilation control strate-
gies are therefore becoming increasingly important with regards to lowering the building
energy consumption and providing a good indoor air quality (IAQ) in order to comply
with the newest requirements. In this project the focus was on future residential buildings
and therefore the requirements for the building class 2020 (BC20) were used. An alter-
native to BC20 are Passive house standards [7] or other sustainable classiﬁcations like
LEED [8] or BREEAM [9]. Looking isolated at Denmark, BC20 is the best candidate for
how the future requirements looks in Denmark because it is made by the Danish Ministry
of Transport, Building and Housing. However currently it does not seems like the BC20
will be made mandatory for new buildings in the near future since there are challenges
in complying with the demands for low inﬁltration rate and primary energy consump-
tion while still ensuring a high daylight factor and indoor climate. Table 3.1 shows the
requirements for ventilation systems according to BC20.
Table 3.1: Requirements to residential ventilation for building class 2020. [6]

==PAGE:30==
3. BACKGROUND
3.2
Market analysis on residential ventilation systems
A market analysis on ventilation systems in Danish multi-family buildings was conducted
by Vanek [10] based on interviews on design practice for residential ventilation answered
by seven ventilation system producers and seven ventilation designers.
The study revealed that ventilation system with heat recovery was by far the most pop-
ular ventilation control strategy among the producers while both natural ventilation and
ventilation system with heat recovery were the most popular among the consultants. Ad-
ditionally the producers had a small preference towards control systems without sensors
compared to control systems with sensors. In contrast the consultants had a small prefer-
ence towards control systems with sensors. Systems with manual override options were
slightly more popular than systems without manual override options for both stakeholder
groups. The popularity of each sensor type mentioned in the interview can be seen in
Figure 3.1. A relative humidity sensor was the preferred sensor type for both producers
and consultants. A combined relative humidity and temperature sensor was the second
most popular sensor for the producers, while a temperature sensor was the second most
popular sensor for consultants.
Figure 3.1: Most popular types of sensors according to producers and consultants (from
[11]).
3.3
Guidelines on residential ventilation systems
The Danish Standard DS 447 [12] and the SBI-guideline 266 [13] describe three venti-
lation methods for residential buildings. Mechanical ventilation, natural ventilation and
hybrid ventilation i.e a combination of the previous two. The SBI-guideline 266 presents
6
==PAGE:31==
3.3. Guidelines on residential ventilation systems
advantages and disadvantages with mechanical ventilation and natural ventilation as well
as challenges in implementing the hybrid ventilation. The advantages and disadvantages
with mechanical and natural ventilation systems proposed by the SBI-guideline 266 can
be seen in Figure 3.2 and 3.3, respectively.
Table 3.2: Advantages and disadvantages with mechanical ventilation systems [13]
Advantages
+ Independent of outdoor conditions and less dependent on user behaviour.
+ Possible to avoid draught.
+ It is possible to reduce the energy used to heat the supply air with the use of heat recovery.
+ Filtration of supply air are possible.
Disadvantages
÷ Noise can occur from ventilation system.
÷ Expenses for maintenance.
÷ Energy-demanding to remove heat on warm days.
÷ Ventilation ducts require space.
Table 3.3: Advantages and disadvantages with natural ventilation systems [13].
Advantages
+ Little energy required to run the ventilation system.
+ System does not create noise.
+ Can easily provide outdoor air to remove excess heat.
Disadvantages
÷ Dependent on outdoor conditions and user behaviour.
÷ Difﬁcult to evaluate if ventilation provided is sufﬁcient to avoid growth of mold and dust mites.
÷ Difﬁcult to ensure sufﬁcient ﬁltration of supply air.
÷ Draught can occur on cold days.
÷ Outside noise can travel trough the fresh air vents.
÷ Does not use heat recovery and more energy is required to heat the supply air.
The hybrid ventilation has the combined beneﬁt of the two systems. A mechanical
system is beneﬁcial in the winter period with the utilisation of heat recovery and the
natural ventilation system is beneﬁcial in the summer period because it does not require
energy to transport air. The challenge is to make an appropriate control strategy that can
run the system in the intermediate period being the transaction period from one system to
the other.
The ventilation control strategy in old residencies has primarily been based on natural
ventilation while new buildings often use hybrid ventilation and mechanical ventilation.
This is both due to the stricter requirements for ventilation but also because new buildings
are very airtight compared to older buildings and thus more mechanical ventilation is
needed in new buildings to compensate for less natural ventilation through leakages in
the building envelope.
7
==PAGE:32==
3. BACKGROUND
3.4
Literature review on research in residential
demand-controlled ventilation
State of the art research in design and operational strategies for energy efﬁcient ventilation
systems are described in this chapter.
Among many of the studies concerning ventilation systems only few focus on residen-
tial buildings [14], even though people spend on average 50-70% of their time at home
[15]. Table 3.4 shows publications on residential demand controlled ventilation (DCV)
from 2000 to 2017. It is shown in the table if the studies used parameter variation and if
the building case corresponds to a low-energy building similar to the Danish BC20. The
control variables are also shown for each of the studies along with the savings reported
on heat and air ﬂow reduction and whether the results are based on simulations (S) or
measurements (M). Out of the 13 publications nine used humidity as a control variable,
seven used occupancy detection and seven used CO2 levels. Only three studies looked
at low-energy residential buildings corresponding to BC20. Hence the potential of DCV
in low-energy residential buildings is not covered in depth in the literature. With the ex-
ception of Van Den Bossche et al. and Laverge et al. none of the studies looked at how
parameter variations inﬂuence the result. This leads to the conclusion that including both
low-energy residential buildings and the inﬂuence of parameter variations when evaluat-
ing DCV has most likely not been done before.

8
==PAGE:33==
3.4. Literature review on research in residential demand-controlled ventilation
studies.
Guglietti and Pesavento [11] investigated 16 different ventilation control strategies,
two cases with constant air volume (CAV) and 14 with variable air volume (VAV) both
balanced and unbalanced cases. IDA ICE software was used as the simulation tool in the
investigation. It is a whole-year dynamic multi-zone simulation tool used to study indoor
climate and energy consumption of buildings [27]. Six of the balanced systems were
controlled by a central sensor placed in the exhaust duct and the sensor and controller
type was what set them apart. The last of the VAV systems was controlled by sensors
located in each room. The sensor types investigated were CO2, temperature and humidity.
The investigation showed that the unbalanced systems were not as energy efﬁcient as the
balanced systems. Generally VAV systems with one single central sensor had a lower
energy consumption than the systems with local sensors, but half of the systems with
central sensors did not comply with the requirements set for the indoor climate. All the
systems with local sensors complied with the indoor climate requirements, but also had a
higher energy consumption as a consequence. The sensor types with the best performance
were CO2 and RH sensors. Furthermore, the CAV systems did not perform as well as the
balanced VAV systems.
Laverge et al. [4] focused on energy saving potential of four demand control strategies
for mechanical ventilation in residential buildings. The robustness of the control strate-
gies was assessed by sensitivity analysis based on the Monte-Carlo method. An average
Belgium dwelling was investigated and heat recovery units were not used in the ventila-
tion system. Three of the control strategies were either controlled by relative humidity
(RH), occupancy presence or CO2. The fourth was controlled by a combination of the
other three control strategies. The strategies using a RH sensor or presence detection
controlled the exhaust ventilation, while a CO2 sensor controlled the supply ventilation.
The results revealed that the strategies using a RH sensor or presence detection had nearly
similar energy saving potential. In comparison the system controlling the supply air with
a CO2 sensor nearly had the same energy saving potential but was a lot more robust.
The systems controlled by either RH, presence (exhaust) or CO2 (supply) had a saving
potential of about 25% compared to a reference case with continuous ﬂow rates. The
fourth ventilation control strategy that combined the previous three systems had a saving
potential of 60% compared to the reference case.
Pavlovas [14] investigated a DCV system for a typical Swedish multifamily building
with exhaust ventilation. Four exhaust ventilation systems were evaluated, a reference
case with CAV and three DCV systems with either CO2, humidity or occupancy con-
trol. Simulation with IDA ICE software and real measurements from the reference case
with CAV revealed that energy savings were possible by using occupancy and/or humid-
ity controlled ventilation. This reduced the average ventilation ﬂow rate while keeping
9
==PAGE:34==
3. BACKGROUND
an acceptable indoor climate. The reference apartment (67.5 m2) was a typical Swedish
apartment from the early 70s with one bedroom, living room, kitchen and combined laun-
dry and bathroom. The exhaust units were placed in the bathroom and kitchen and out-
door air was supplied by air inlets in the bedroom and living room. Internal loads from
occupants, equipment, cooking, water vapour emission from food cooking, showering,
cloth drying and occupants were accounted for. The reference case extracted 15 l/s from
both the bathroom and living room, corresponding to 0.44 l/s pr. m2. In the apartments
with DCV the exhaust airﬂow varied from a base airﬂow of 10 l/s to 30 l/s. The simulated
results from the reference case with a constant air volume ﬂow rate of 0.44 l/s pr. m2 had
a reasonable CO2 and RH levels below 1200 ppm and 70%, respectively. The reference
case was simulated in two modes, with all doors open or all doors closed. The results
showed that open or closed doors had a large inﬂuence on the indoor climate. There was
risk of increased RH in the case were all the doors were open, because of humidity trans-
port from the bathroom to the rest of the apartment. As a consequence relative humidity
could reach 90% in the summertime. Windows were assumed closed all year round and as
the authors pointed out this is especially not realistic in the summertime were occupants
frequently open windows. Hence the 90% RH would probably not occur that frequent
in reality. The CO2 and RH control strategies showed an approximate 50% reduction in
annual energy demand for ventilation (depending on the setpoint), while the occupancy
control strategy results in approximately 20% reduction. This was in comparison to the
reference case with CAV. The indoor climate was for all cases regarded as acceptable.
3.5
Sensitivity analysis
The purpose of a sensitivity analysis is to study the uncertainty in the output of a model
due to different sources of uncertainty in the inputs to the model. A sensitivity analysis
can quantify how important different input parameters are to the output. In addition it can
quantify how the output of a speciﬁc parameter change is affected by the assembly of the
other parameters in the model.
Sensitivity analysis can be grouped into three categories: screening methods, local
sensitivity methods and global sensitivity methods [28]. Screening methods are advanta-
geous for advanced models with many parameters and thus models that require a lot of
computational power. It can identify and rank qualitatively the parameters that inﬂuence
the output the most. For each parameter two extreme values are selected, a minimum
value and maximum value. Each parameter is changed once and the change is compared
to a reference model.
10
==PAGE:35==
3.5. Sensitivity analysis
The local sensitivity method is also based on changing one parameter at the time and
calculate how much the change inﬂuence the output. It is assumed that the input and
output relation is linear and thus the correlation with other parameters are not taken into
consideration.
In the global sensitivity method output variation due to a parameter is evaluated by
varying the other parameters as well. The probability density function of each input
parameter is also included in this method. The inﬂuence of a parameter not only relies on
the distribution and range of the individual parameter but also on the inﬂuence by the other
parameters [28]. The global sensitivity method can quantify how the output of a speciﬁc
parameter change is affected by the assembly of the other parameters in the model. This
is especially important for the evaluation of buildings where the overall performance is
important.
The global sensitivity method can be performed in a lot of different ways and differ-
ent studies have tried to evaluate these methods when applied to building performance.
Nguyen and Reiter [29] evaluated 9 different sensitivity methods, Yang et al. [30] com-
pared 4 methods and Brohus et al. [31] also compared 4 methods. There was not a clear
consensus on which method was the best but a method called the Morris method was
evaluated as a reasonable method that is advantageous when a lot of parameters are to
be investigated. It is not the most accurate of all the methods but it is computational less
demanding and has an acceptable ability in quantifying sensitivity and parameter interac-
tion.
Aner Martinez and Mark F. [32] investigated how uncertain the results from building
simulation models are to the multiple sources of uncertainty in the input parameters. This
was done by the use of sensitivity and uncertainty analysis. The study found that the
uncertainty in the residential energy models are large with a range of -24% to +54% in
relation to a model with mean input values. This emphasises the importance of sensitivity
and uncertainty analysis when evaluating energy consumption of residencies.
Evaluation of sensitivity analyses used in the literature
In the recent years multiple publications have used sensitivity analysis to better under-
stand the energy use of residential buildings. Most of the studies using sensitivity anal-
ysis have not focused on the ventilation system and the IAQ and very few of them have
investigated low-energy buildings equivalent to e.g the Danish building class 2020. Bro-
hus et al. [31] investigated the inﬂuence occupant behaviour has on energy consump-
tion of residential buildings and a ranking of parameters was performed using sensitivity
analysis. Natural ventilation was used as the ventilation method and it was not a low-
energy building. Molin et al. [33] investigated the energy performance of a newly built
11
==PAGE:36==
3. BACKGROUND
low-energy buildings in Sweden with a parametric study. The study used a balanced me-
chanical ventilation system with CAV and ventilation heat recovery (VHR). Loannou and
Itard [34] used sensitivity analysis to analyse how parameters related to building and oc-
cupancy behaviour affected the energy consumption and PMV comfort index. The study
did not vary the ventilation control strategy but focused on the heating systems. One of
the studied cases was a low-energy building. Facundo [35] used sensitivity analysis in
combination with simulation based optimisation to optimise the thermal and energy per-
formance of a residential building in Argentine. The building was naturally ventilated.
Pinto et al. [36] evaluated mixed ventilation in a typical Portuguese residential apartment
building with the use of sensitivity analysis. It was not a low-energy building and the
ventilation systems investigated were fairly simple with mechanical exhaust only in the
kitchen.
This leads to the conclusion that a sensitivity analysis has not been used to analyse
how different building related parameters inﬂuence the energy consumption and indoor
climate in low-energy buildings with different mechanical ventilation control strategies.
3.6
Objectives
The aim of the Master’s thesis was to conduct a sensitivity analysis on advanced ventila-
tion control strategies for residences suggested in the earlier stage of the IEA-EBC Annex
68 project [1].
The main objective of the present study was to investigate which control strategy for me-
chanical ventilation performs the best with regards to primary energy consumption and in-
door climate when evaluated with a sensitivity analysis. Further it was investigated which
control strategy for mechanical ventilation in low-energy buildings is the least sensitive
to parameter variations and which building-related parameter has the largest inﬂuence on
the performance of ventilation systems in low-energy residences.
3.7
Hypothesis
The hypothesis was that an advanced ventilation system in residential buildings with lo-
cal humidity, CO2 and temperature sensors can provide better indoor climate at a lower
energy consumption compared to less advanced ventilation systems.

4.1
Case description
This study builds upon the same apartment used by Guglietti and Pesavento [11] which
was based on projects by Zilio [37] and Sarran [38]. The apartment is placed in Nordhavn,
Copenhagen, near the waterfront and the total ﬂoor area of the apartment is 93.8 m2 and
consists of four rooms with a combined living room and kitchen (LR), two bathroom and
a small depot (see Figure 4.1).

As an addition to Table 4.1 district heating was modelled to supply a temperature of
70◦C during the summer period and supply 79◦C during the winter period. This was based
on reported values for Nordhavn which is covered by HOFOR A/S [39]. There is no need
for space heating during the summer according to [40], but often window-opening and
space heating will counteract each other during the summer and therefore a heat setback
during the summer was needed. The model was setup with a summer heat setback of 5
month from 1st of May to 30th of September. The lighting system used in the IDA ICE
model was based on work done by Sarran [38]. The light is controlled by illuminance
intensity, turning the light fully on when the level is below 250 lux and does this in all
rooms except the kitchen where the lower limit is 350 lux. The deadband is 100 lux.
When a room is occupied the light turns on with the exception of the bedroom where the
light always turns off during the night. In the IDA ICE model the zone model ﬁdelity was

==PAGE:39==
4.1. Case description
selected to be Energy over the other type, Climate. The zone model ﬁdelity of Climate is
the most precise with extended calculation for e.g. the vertical temperature difference but
it is only functioning for rectangular rooms and therefore it could not be used.
Dimensioning of the AHU
Dimensioning of the air handling unit (AHU) is important when evaluating the ventilation
control strategies because it set a limit for how much air can be supplied. The size of the
AHU was designed according to three design scenarios, moisture load, CO2 concentration
and requirements deﬁned in standards. Since the model setup allows for window-opening
the thermal environment has not been a dimensioning factor for the AHU.
Scenario I: Dimensioning the AHU in matter of moisture production is done for the
maximum production of moisture (Gv,p) in a typical residence which corresponds to 1.32
kg/h derived from the literature described in detail in subsection 4.5. The dimensioning
of the AHU according to moisture load can be done in multiple ways. The demands
which was setup in this project was for the maximum requirements for category II in
DS/EN 15251 [41] of 60% for the normal minimum dimensioning value for indoor air
temperature of 20◦C. The ambient temperature and RH have a large inﬂuence on the
needed ventilation capacity (Qvent) [m3/h] . The higher the temperature and/or the RH of
the ambient air the higher is the needed ventilation capacity and therefore it is important to
balance the ambient condition so you have sufﬁcient capacity without overdimensioning
the AHU. In this dimensioning of the AHU an ambient temperature of 5◦C and RH of
95% were used. Before Qvent could be calculated when dimensioning for moisture, the
saturation vapour pressure (pvsat) [Pa] need to be calculated as in equation 4.1.
pvsat(θ) = 288.68

tion 4.2.

4. METHODS

where Rv is the water vapour gas constant [J/kg/K], Gv,p is the moisture production [kg/h],
θi is the indoor air temperature [◦C], pv,i is the demand and indoor actual vapour pressure
[Pa] and pv,e is the exterior water pressure [Pa].
Scenario II: The dimensioning ventilation capacity for the indoor air quality (QCO2,vent)
[m3/h], measured by CO2 concentration was based on the requirements from category II
in DS/EN 15251 of 500 ppm above background concentration [41]. The default value for
the background concentration in IDA ICE is 400 ppm which is a commonly used value
for the background concentration resulting in a maximum limit of 900 ppm. To calculate
QCO2,vent, equation 4.4 was used. The required ventilation capacity from this calculation
method can be seen in table 4.2.
QCO2,vent = n · GCO2,p
C(∞)
=
n · GCO2,p
CTot −Cback
[m3/h]
(4.4)
where n is the number of persons, GCO2,p is the production of CO2 per person [ml/h/person],
C(∞) is the steady state concentration without background concentration, CTot is the in-
door maximum concentration [ppm] and Cback is the background outdoor concentration
[ppm].
Scenario III: The last way of calculating the dimensioning ventilation capacity was
done by using the general requirements deﬁned in the standards. The Danish building
regulation, DS 447 [12] was used as a reference to how the ventilation system need to
be setup. In Annex C there is a calculation method which is a supplementary way to the
building regulations way of deﬁning the dimensioning ventilation capacity. The dimen-
sioning method according to the standard is a simple and often dimensions the AHU on
the safe side where all sources for bad indoor environment in a standard scenario should
be covered. The calculation method for category II in DS 447 was used as the last way of
dimensioning the ventilation capacity (qDS,vent) [l/s] where each person needs 7 l/s and
for each square meter ﬂoor area at least 0.7 l/s needs to be delivered. The dimensioning
ventilation capacity was then determined by equation 4.5.

4.2
Evaluation of the ventilation control strategies
The indoor climate was evaluated by three parameters, the thermal environment by oper-
ative temperature, the indoor air quality by CO2 concentration (ppm) and the humidity by
relative humidity.
Guidance on maximum limits for CO2 concentration and humidity was found in in
DS/EN 15251 [41]. Category II in DS/EN 15251 has a maximum limit for CO2 concen-
tration of 900 ppm and the limit for RH is 60%. There are three categories to choose from
in DS/EN 15251 and category II was used instead of category I and III because category
II is for new building where category I is for buildings with sensitive persons and high ex-
pectation for the indoor climate, while category III is for existing buildings. The Danish
17
==PAGE:42==
4. METHODS
building regulation BR18 (§385-§392) [6] requirements to the thermal environment is that
it is allowed having a maximum of 100 hours above 27 ◦C and maximum 25 hours above
28 ◦C. By this approach more than one value is used to evaluate the thermal environment
(hours above 27 ◦C and hours above 28◦C). In a sensitivity analysis it is preferred to only
have one value for each evaluation parameters in order to simplify the evaluation and get
a better overview.
Another way of evaluating the thermal environment is proposed by DS/EN 15251 An-
nex F, using degree hours. It draws similarities with the BR18 requirements by evaluating
both on hours with overheating and the degree of overheating but it summarises it into
one value. Equation 4.6 shows how degree hours (Dh) [◦C · h] was calculated and Fig-
ure 4.2 shows how it was implemented in IDA ICE for each zone. As an example, two
occupied hours with an average of 3 ◦C above 27 ◦C gives a total of 6 degree hours. The
IAQ and humidity were evaluated by the same principle. The IAQ is evaluated in CO2
hours (CO2h) [ppm · h] which is the number of occupied hours where the CO2 concentra-
tion is above 900 ppm multiplied with the difference between the CO2 concentration and
the maximum limit of 900 ppm. The humidity was evaluated by relative humidity hours
(RHh) [% · h] which is the number of hours where the RH is above 60% multiplied with
the difference between the RH and the maximum limit of 60%.
Dh =
Z t2
t1
T(t) −Tlimit dt
[◦C · h]
(4.6)
where t1 is the time when overheating occur, t2 is the time when the temperature is below
limit again, T(t) is the operative indoor temperature over time and Tlimit is the tempera-
ture limit.
For the temperature and CO2 concentration the evaluation was made for living spaces
which in the guidance to BR18 (§368-§376) [6] is deﬁned as accommodations or rooms
where you sleep and not small delimited rooms as bathrooms, entry etc. Therefore de-
gree hours and CO2 hours is the difference between the exceeding value and the limit
integrated over the occupied hours in the three bedrooms and the living room. The RH
hours does not only affect the occupants well being but also the building conditions. The
RH hours is therefore the offset above the maximum RH integrated over the associated
hours for both the three bedrooms, living room and the two bathrooms, independent of
occupancy presence. To avoid overestimating humidity hours in the bathrooms compared
to the same scenario in the combined living room and kitchen the humidity hours is a
weighed value to the ﬂoor area. The weighted factors can be seen in Table 4.3.
The primary energy consumption is more straightforward to evaluate compared to the
indoor climate parameters, it is merely the primary energy consumption over a year. Pri-

mary energy factors are used according to the Building class 2020, with a factor of 0.6
for district heating and 1.8 for electricity. The energy consumption for equipment and
lightning are not taken into account. In addition the energy for domestic hot water con-
sumption is not taken into account. Domestic hot water can be a large fraction of the total
energy consumption especially in low-energy residencies. The total energy consumption
calculated for each model and the energy for the domestic hot water should therefore be
added to get the actual primary energy consumption as deﬁned in the Danish building
regulation.
To evaluate an investigation of several models there is a need for only having one
overall value summarising the results for each model for both the thermal environment,
CO2 concentration, RH and primary energy consumption relative to the other models.
This can be done by ﬁrst weighting the primary energy consumption, degree hours, CO2
hours or RH hours with the average value of the results from all the models used in
the investigation which need to be evaluated. It is calculated in the same way for both the
primary energy consumption weighted with the average (Eavg), the degree hours weighted
with the average (Dhavg), the CO2 hours weighted with the average (CO2havg) and the
RH hours weighted with the average (RHhavg). In equation 4.7 the calculation for the
Eavg is shown and the same method applies for the other evaluation parameters.
19

Ei
E1,E2,...,En
n
[−]
(4.7)
Where Eavg,i is the weighted energy consumption for model i to the average energy con-
sumption for all the investigated models, Ei is the energy consumption for model i and n
is the total number of investigated models.
The average value for each of the four weighted values can be used as the one evalu-
ation parameter in the investigation, but to avoid overestimating the three indoor climate
parameter these are weighted into one value also referred to as the weighed indoor cli-
mate (I) . This result is an overall value referred to as the Energy and Indoor climate
weighted value to the average (EI) which was used as the overall evaluation of which
model perform the best. The calculation of EI can be seen in equation 4.8.
EI = Eavg,i + Ii

Where Eavg,i, Dhavg,i, CO2havg,i and RHhavg,i is respectively the weighted primary en-
ergy consumption, degree hours, CO2 hours and RH hours for model i to the average of
the respectively values of all the investigated models and Ii is the average of the three
weighted indoor climate parameters for model i.
4.3
Ventilation control strategies
Most of the ventilation control strategies tested in this study were based on work done
by Guglietti and Pesavento [11], who used the results from a survey by Vanek [10] to
build several ventilation control strategies. Guglietti and Pesavento found some ventila-
tion control strategies performing much worse than the rest and these ventilation control
strategies were not tested in this study due to their poor performance. Eight ventilation
control strategies were selected from the work by Guglietti and Pesavento and addition-
ally two new strategies were added. Table 4.4 shows all the ventilation control strategies
to be investigated and their setpoint values.
4.4
Selection of design parameters
There are many design parameters that inﬂuence the efﬁciency of a ventilation system
used in residencies and thus prioritising is important when choosing the ones to be inves-
tigated in the sensitivity analysis. In order to make an informed decision on which design

parameters to include, the studies introduced in chapter 3.5 were used as guidance. Rank-
ing the inﬂuence of design parameters directly to the efﬁciency of a ventilation system
used in low-energy building had not been done in any of the studies. Thus the results
from the studies can not be applied directly to what parameters have the largest inﬂuence
in this study, but they can still be used as an indication.
Brohus et al. [31] investigated the inﬂuence of occupant behaviour on energy con-
sumption of residential buildings. A ranking of design parameters was performed using
global sensitivity analysis, see Figure 4.3. The setpoint for space heating has the high-
est ranking, while the second most important factor is occupancy heat load, followed by
natural ventilation and inﬁltration, appliance heat load and the occupied period.
The study by Rackes and Waring [42] was the only identiﬁed study to investigate
the performance of different ventilation systems with a sensitivity analysis. The study
1Ventilation rate adjust to the control parameter in three steps from minimum to medium to maximum
ventilation rate
2Ventilation rate gradually adjust according to the control parameter
21
==PAGE:46==
4. METHODS
Figure 4.3: Ranking of parameters that inﬂuence the energy consumption of residential
buildings. [31]
was done for ofﬁces and not residential buildings. 17 parameters were investigated by
the Monte Carlo method. To rank the inﬂuence of the parameters they used a weighted
sensitivity index (WSI) that combined results for all the ventilation control strategies by
averaging their sensitivity index. The sensitivity index is deﬁned as the square of the
standardised regression coefﬁcient. For a small ofﬁce with CAV the top three parameters
are occupancy density (WSI = 26%), followed by heating degree days (WSI = 13%) and
inﬁltration rate (WSI = 8%). For a medium sized ofﬁce with VAV the top ﬁve parameters
are heating setpoint (WSI = 17%), heating degree days (WSI = 14%), unoccupied period
zone setpoint setback (WSI = 13%), cooling coefﬁcient of performance (WSI = 9%), zone
cooling setpoint (WSI = 7%). The building envelope only had minor inﬂuence, except that
heat gain from solar radiation through windows had some inﬂuence.
The heating degree days and setback are not relevant for this projects sensitivity anal-
ysis. This study focuses on Denmark where one weather ﬁle to a large degree can rep-
resent the entire country because of the limited change of weather across the country in
contrary to the USA where Rackes and Waring has made their investigation. The setback
is more common to use in ofﬁce buildings since a whole weekend often is unoccupied.
The cooling system and setpoint can not directly be investigated due to the absence of
a cooling system. Cooling is not commonly used in Danish residences, occupants are
instead dependent on window-opening, night ventilation and supply of ambient air.
Molin et al. [33] investigated the energy performance of newly built low-energy build-
ings in Sweden with a parametric study. The ventilation method was a balanced CAV
system with a VHR unit. When cold bridges or inﬁltration rate were doubled, the space
heating demand increased by approximately 40%. Lowering the inﬁltration rate by half,
decreased the demand for space heating by 14%. Doubling the internal heat gain to 8.8
W/m2 decreased the demand for space heating by 86%. Changing the occupancy load
from 0 to 6 persons, decreased the demand for space heating by 55%. Increasing the
ventilation heat recovery efﬁciency from 76% to 86%, reduced space heating by 11%.
22
==PAGE:47==
4.4. Selection of design parameters
Increasing the UA value by 10%, decreased the space heating demand by 14%. Wind
speed, thermal mass and g-value (E and W orientated windows) inﬂuenced the energy
demand with less than 5%.
Dodoo et al. [43] included the ventilation heat recovery efﬁciency as a design param-
eter and found that increasing the VHR from 70% to 76% reduced the energy for space
heating by 28% and with a VHR of 85% the saving was 33% compared to the VHR of
70%. The study also changed the setpoint from 23 ◦C to 20 ◦C and that resulted in a 25%
energy saving for space heating. By increasing the person heat gain from 1.68 W/m2 to
4.30 W/m2 cooling demand would increase 133% and heating decreased by 17%. In-
creasing the electrical heat gain from 2.40 W/m2 to 4.92 W/m2 increased cooling demand
by 100% and decreased the heat demand by 18%.
Laverge et al. [4] used a sensitivity analysis to evaluate the energy saving potential
of four demand control strategies for mechanical ventilation in residential buildings. The
sensitivity analysis was based on the Monte-Carlo approach and revealed inﬁltration rate,
wind related factors, number of occupants and their schedules to have the largest inﬂuence
on the overall performance of the ventilation systems.
The inﬁltration rate showed a high degree of inﬂuence in a lot of the studies however
it should be emphasised that the studies where it had a lot of inﬂuence were either based
on older houses or foreign houses not subject to the Danish building regulations. The
maximum allowed inﬁltration rate in a BC20 residence is a lot lower than for the studies
being discussed. To comply with the inﬁltration rate of a BC20 residence or bring it even
further down is very difﬁcult and therefore the inﬁltration rate was excluded as a design
parameter in the sensitivity analysis. Both Molin et al. and Dodoo et al. found the VHR
to have a large inﬂuence. What separates the two studies from this study is the stricter
requirements from BC20 that require VHR to have at least 85% efﬁciency. This makes
the range of values to pick from very narrow compared to Molin et al. and Dodoo et al.,
hence it will most likely not have a large inﬂuence in this study. Therefore this design
parameter was not used in the sensitivity analysis. The building envelope of a BC20
residence need to have a very low heat loss through cold bridges, walls, foundation and
roofs. The building envelope in BC20 is for that reason thick which narrows the range to
vary this design parameter thus the envelope of the apartment was not a part of this study.
Rackes and Waring also found the building envelope to have minor inﬂuence in their
study. Based on the knowledge from these studies the following ten design parameters in
table 4.5 have been selected for the sensitivity analysis.

Design parameters and probability density function
In order to make the sensitivity analysis it was necessary to set up a probability density
function (PDF) for each design parameter. Before the PDF could be deﬁned the range
of the values for each design parameters and the distribution type needs to be deﬁned.
An overview of the design parameters range of values, distribution type, mean-value µ
and standard deviation σ of the probability density function can be seen in Table 4.6. For
some of the design parameters the PDF was determined by actual data sets or by stochastic
models, hence their range and distribution values are not in Table 4.6. For each design
parameters a further description of the range of values and how they were determined can
be seen in this Section on page 25 to 36.

4.5. Design parameters and probability density function
Setpoint for space heating
Andersen [44] investigated temperature setpoint in 56 Danish dwellings over a period of
two months and the measurements were logged every 5 minute. Based on the temperature
distribution of each 56 dwellings an average temperature distribution were derived. By
contacting the author it was possible to get the data from this average temperature distri-
bution which could be used further on in the sensitivity analysis as values for the setpoint
for space heating.
Occupancy schedule
Multiple occupancy schedules were made in order to create a distribution used later in
the sensitivity analysis. This was done by creating a stochastic occupancy schedule gen-
erator based on raw data from two related studies [45, 46]. The two studies were based
on a Danish time use survey from 2008/09 [48]. A total of 17,707 individuals from 4679
households were part of the survey and their activity were logged in 10-minutes incre-
ments throughout the day for all weekdays. Barthelmes et al. [45] used these data to
make daily activity proﬁles where the probability of an individual doing a certain activity
at a speciﬁc time of a day was deﬁned. Figure 4.4 shows the daily activity proﬁle from
the study.
Figure 4.4: Daily activity proﬁles [45].
25
==PAGE:50==
4. METHODS
Barthelmes et al. [46] used the data to make survival functions based on the Kaplan-
Meier survival analysis method [49] for daily occupants activities, see ﬁgure 4.5. The
Y-axis shows the S(t) values which is the number of activities started subtracted from the
number of activities ﬁnished divided by the number of activities started, the X-axis shows
the time.
Figure 4.5: Kaplan-Meier procedure for daily occupants activities [46].
The amount of people in the apartment should also be included as a variable in the
schedule since it deﬁnes where the tenants stay. The amount of people also inﬂuences
the occupancy load which is another parameter investigated. Since the amount of tenants
hardly can be separated from the occupancy schedule the schedule was made with differ-
ent amount of people in the apartment. The number of tenants in the apartment inﬂuences
the distribution of tenants in each apartment room and the activity behaviour. As an ex-
ample, two adults and three children in the apartment are assumed to result in two adults
in one bedroom, two children in the second bedroom and one child in the last bedroom.
One adult and two children are assumed to result in one adult in one bedroom, one child in
the second bedroom and the last child in the last bedroom. Activity in rooms is dependent
on occupancy of the room which makes the activity dependent on the schedule.
The Danish statistical authorities [50] was contacted in order to get data on the number
of resident in a four room apartment. The data collected was the number of residents in
a four room apartments and how many of these were children. The data on amount of
adults and children made the distribution of the tenants in the bedrooms more precise.

With the raw data from [45, 46, 50] it was possible to build a random occupancy
schedule generator in Excel. Apart from the three raw input parts the generator consist
of four calculation parts. The ﬁrst calculation part is with logic statements and a random
number generator deﬁning what activity is occurring. In total there are 10 different types
of activities: Sleeping, toilette, eating, cooking, cleaning/washing clothes, practical work,
family care, relaxing/IT, not at home and other. In the generator the activity is evaluated
with intervals of 10 minutes during a whole day and for each new activity a ﬁxed duration
is set based on the survival function [46].
The schedule generator needed a starting point during the day to begin the ﬁrst activity.
Initially midnight or in the morning seamed to be a reasonable starting point but it was
later changed to 8 pm since the activity sleep would often be cut off if the starting point
was at midnight. Starting the loop in the morning does not work either since there is no
clear and long enough transition between sleeping and not at home resulting in a large
risk of interfering with an activity.
Special logical scripts were used when the probability was above 5% that an activity
last for more than a quarter of a day. The activity where this is typical is sleeping, Family
care, relaxing and not at home. The special script only allows one long duration of the
activity to occur per day to avoid only those types of activities to occur during a day. If
the activity occurs a second time it is limited to a duration of 30 minutes.
This process of creating a daily schedule with different activities was repeated seven
times to simulate up to seven different persons. A more detailed description of the ﬁrst
calculation part can be found in the Sixth Appendix.
The second calculation part converts the results from the ﬁrst part to a values that can
be entered into the IDA ICE models. If an activity occurs the output is 1 and 0 if the
activity does not occur. For each of the 10 activities a daily schedule with intervals of 10
minutes is made except the activity relaxing/IT which is divided into four schedules, one
27
==PAGE:52==
4. METHODS
for each bedroom and one for the living room. This is necessary because the schedule for
relaxing/IT is used to control the equipment load from IT. It was assumed that 30% of the
time a person uses IT equipment it occurs in their bedroom instead of the living room.
The third calculation part takes the household type and size into account and distribute
the right amount of people into the different rooms. First the household types are deﬁned
between single adult or a couple combined with how many children there are as the dis-
tribution shown in Table 4.7. Afterwards the chosen number of persons are distributed
into the rooms after what activity they are doing. The assumptions taken and how the
distribution of each activity is described in Table 4.8.

==PAGE:53==
4.5. Design parameters and probability density function
The toilet activity has a very low probability of occurring during the day which re-
sulted in a lot of schedules without toilet activity. This is not realistic and was ﬁxed by
allowing toilet activity to temporarily interrupt any occurring activity until the toilet ac-
tivity was over. Some schedules did not have any cooking activity during the day and to
make it more realistic a cooking activity of 30 minutes was inserted from 5.30 pm to 6
pm. The 30 minute duration is based on Barthelmes et al. [46] and Madindeks [51].
With the stochastic occupancy schedule generator 21 schedules was created which
was the data-set for the distribution used in the sensitivity analysis. The distribution of
the 21 schedules was according to how many occupancy hours each schedule had.
Window-opening
Advanced stochastic models are becoming more popular and a study by Andersen et al.
[52] describes a stochastic model for window-opening. The study was based on mea-
surements from 15 danish dwellings over an eight month period. From experience the
use of stochastic models makes the simulation time 5-10 times longer and for this reason
stochastic models was not used to model window-opening.
The window-opening angle was used as the variable in the sensitivity analysis. The
temperature setpoint for the window-opening was not chosen as the variable because this
will interfere with the evaluation parameter degree hours. A recent study by Henrik-
sen and Olesen [47] modelled the occupants window-opening behaviour in residential
buildings. Data on window-opening angles of 179 windows from 37 different residences
located in Copenhagen were monitored in a period of 9-12 months. The data-set for the
bedroom window-opening angle and the living room window-opening angle was used to
make the distribution used in the sensitivity analysis. The IDA ICE software require two
input values in order to simulate the window-opening. The two input values are the effec-
tive area and the Discharge Coefﬁcient. A study by Hult et al. [53] found a relationship
between window-opening angle and the effective area and the discharge coefﬁcient. This
relationship was used to convert the opening angle into values the IDA ICE software can
use.
The control of the windows was setup as a macro1 in IDA ICE to make sure every
part of the control work as intended, see Figure 4.6. It consist of four parts where the
ﬁrst part is closing the window when the ambient air temperature is above the indoor
air temperature because it was assumed the occupants will not open the windows when
this occurs. The second part is deﬁning the effective opening area which is the variable
design parameter in the sensitivity analysis. Since the night ventilation also was a design
1A macro is a method in the IDA ICE software used to create new ways of processing data in the
software which can be used to a lot of things e.g to create new control strategies
29
==PAGE:54==
4. METHODS
parameter in the sensitivity analysis the window-opening parameter was limited to the
daytime and this was controlled in a control scheme. The third and fourth part are added
in an additional macro which can be seen in Figure 4.7 as the day control macro. The
third part is the actual control which is a P-controller because it makes the simulation run
faster compared to on/off control. The window begin to open at 23 ◦C which is inspired
by the Danish building energy classiﬁcation program, BE15. The window-opens with the
maximum deﬁned effective area at 27 ◦C since it is deﬁned as the ﬁrst temperature limit in
BR18. The fourth part activates the control when the zone with the window is occupied.
Figure 4.6: The IDA ICE macro for the window-opening used in the living room. The
day control macro is shown in Figure 4.7
Because of some difﬁculties running the models the ﬁrst order component was imple-
mented to increase the models capable of successfully complete the simulation. Another
challenge was the fact that the setpoint of 23◦C for window-opening is below some of the
heating setpoints during the heating season. This was solved by adding a new setpoint
during heating season of 23◦C or 0.5◦C above the setpoint for space heating. This was
added as a separate day control macro seen in Figure 4.7 which is connected into the
macro in Figure 4.6.
To reduce the simulation time a proportional temperature control was used to control
the window-opening.
Occupancy load
The occupancy load was varied by changing the metabolic rate (met) of the tenants. The
met is changing from each room type since it vary based on the type of activity occurring
in the different room types. The typical values for the bedrooms, bathrooms and com-
bined living room and kitchen were taken from Smith and Svendsen [54]. Guglietti and
30
==PAGE:55==
4.5. Design parameters and probability density function
Figure 4.7: The day control macro connected into the IDA ICE window-opening macro.
Pesavento [11] made the assumption that the metabolic rate was of equal size for chil-
dren and adults and this simpliﬁcation was also assumed in this study. The minimum and
maximum met values were found in ISO 8996 - Table A.2 and B.3 [55]. The conversion
factor of 1 met=58.2 W/m2 was used to convert the unit wattage per square meter to the
unit met. These values was used to create the PDF used in the sensitivity analysis.
Moisture load
There are many sources of moisture emissions to the air in a residence and they differ
in the amount of moisture they emit and the room they are located inside. Literature
about moisture emission from different activities to the indoor air are scarce. Smith and
Svendsen described a minimum, typical and maximum moisture emission load from dif-
ferent activities, which was based on a study by Yik et al. [56] and Hite and Bray [57].
The moisture emitting activities that is included in the model is cooking, dish washing,
cleaning (ﬂoor mopping), cloth washing and shower load. The actual moisture emission
and distribution for each type is described on page 31 to 33 in their own subsections.
Moisture emission from respiration of occupants is automatically included in the simula-
tion program when setting up occupancy load.
Cooking
The cooking load was separated into three categories, breakfast, lunch and dinner based
on values from Smith and Svendsen [54]. Each category has it own probability density
function which can be seen in ﬁgure 4.6. For most households, dinner is the most moisture
31
==PAGE:56==
4. METHODS
emitting meal, after that comes lunch and the least emitting is breakfast [54]. In some
cases breakfast can be just as moisture emitting as dinner and vice versa depending on
what is cooked. This behaviour can be emulated by having the same range of values for
the three cooking categories and only changing the typical value to correspond to the most
likely values of the given category. Based on values from Smith and Svendsen breakfast
was assigned a typical value of 0.31 kg/meal, lunch 0.41 kg/meal and dinner 0.96 kg/meal.
How often and the duration of cooking was based on the generated occupancy schedule.
When the activity Cooking take place between 10 p.m. to 11 a.m. it will be assigned the
breakfast cooking load, cooking between 11 a.m. - 4 p.m. was assigned the lunch load
and cooking between 4 p.m. - 10 p.m. is assigned the dinner load. This is inspired by a
household survey by Yik et al. [56] that investigated the probability of a person cooking
breakfast, lunch or dinner at certain times of the day.
Dish washing
Modern dishwashers dry the dishes as part of the washing program and the vapour con-
densate on the interior surface of the dishwasher. Hence the moisture emission from
modern dishwashers is very low [54]. The minimal, typical and maximum value for dish
washing are set to 0.05, 0.144, 0.45 kg/day respectively, according to Smith and Svendsen
[54].
Cleaning
Cleaning load from ﬂoor mopping was based on values from Smith and Svendsen and
the drying time was set to 20 minutes based on a study by Yik et al. [56]. It is assumed
that the moisture emission is uniform from when the ﬂoor have just been mopped to it
being completely dry. It was also assumed that ﬂoor mopping occurs once pr. month on
a Saturday at 10 a.m.
Cloth drying
There are multiple ways and locations to dry clothes for tenants who lives in an apartment
building. This needs to be understood in order to simulate the behaviour of the average
person. The process of washing clothes in a washing machine does not emit any substan-
tial moisture as described by Yik et al.. If not done in a tumble dryer clothes drying can
be a large source of moisture. A survey from 2007 [58] showed that approximate 50% of
Danish households have a tumble dryer making it a common method of drying clothes.
It was assumed that drying clothes outside is not that common in Denmark, due to the
Danish weather conditions most of the year does not provide good conditions for drying
clothes outside. It is not uncommon for Danish apartment buildings to have shared rooms
32
==PAGE:57==
4.5. Design parameters and probability density function
for clothes drying. Based on this it was assumed most people living in apartment build-
ings does not dry clothes inside the apartment, but use a tumble dryer or shared rooms in
the apartment building dedicated for clothes drying. In order to reﬂect this behaviour it
was assumed that three out of ﬁve apartments does not dry clothes inside. One out of ﬁve
apartment was assumed to dry clothes inside and emit 1.6 kg moisture per clothes wash
based on Yik et al. [56], while one out of ﬁve also dry clothes inside but emit 2.9 kg mois-
ture pr. clothes wash based on Angell [59]. The amount of moisture emitted from clothes
drying decay during the drying process and this have been documented by Yik et al. and
was included in the sensitivity analysis. It was assumed that for cases with clothes drying
inside it will occur inside bathroom 1 once pr. week each Sunday.
Shower
The shower load was based on an average moisture emission rate which was based on
two literature studies. Angell found 0.25 kg moisture was released from a 5 min shower.
Yik et al. measured a total moisture release of 0.53 kg for a shower length of 18 min.
The average emission thus becomes 0.043 kg/min. The variable for the shower parameter
used in the sensitivity analysis was shower length. The minimum shower length was
assumed to be 5 minutes (0.22 kg/shower). The typical shower length was 8 minutes
(0.35 kg/shower) based on a study by Unilever [60], that determined the average shower
length in the UK to be 8 minutes. The maximum shower length was assumed to be 20
minutes (0.86 kg/shower). For each occupant in the simulation model a shower will occur
once pr. day. This was based on a Danish study from 2009 [61] where it was found that
most Danes take a shower 6-7 times a week corresponding to approximate once per day.
The time of day the shower occur was based on the speciﬁc occupancy schedules for toilet
activity. If it was possible the shower would be placed between 5 a.m. and 9 a.m. or in
the evening between 5 p.m. and 9 p.m..
Internal load
Multiple internal load sources were created for the apartment investigated and this section
will describe the assumption and how the distribution and values was setup.
A minimum, typical and maximum value for the internal load sources were estimated
based on literature on the topic. Zimmermann et al. [63] conducted a household electricity
survey of 251 households in England over the duration of a year. They registered 116
different appliances and rated them according to their occurrence in residencies. Table
4.9 shows which of the appliance from the UK study that was included in the sensitivity
analysis. The selected appliances are the most common in the households. Washing
33
==PAGE:58==
4. METHODS
machines and dishwashers was not included because it was assumed that most of the heat
is release into the drain.
As a simpliﬁcation cooker and extractor hood is grouped together as one internal load
source, because they often work in parallel. The fridge-freezer, router and set-top box
were also grouped together because they are active all year and most commonly placed
in the kitchen or living room. The DVD, TV and laptop were also grouped together
because they are often used when residents participate in the activity relaxing/TV/IT. The
vacuum cleaner, washing machine and tumble dryer are not used as much as the other
appliances and they cannot be linked to one speciﬁc activity in the occupancy schedule.
They were setup in the simulation models as occurring once per week. Table 4.9 shows
the minimum, typical and maximum value of each home appliance and the source which
the values is based upon.

Similar to the moisture load, the internal load from cooking was separated into three
categories, breakfast, lunch and dinner. Activities that takes place between 10 p.m. to 11
a.m. was assigned the breakfast cooking load, 11 a.m. - 4 p.m. was assigned the lunch
load and activities between 4 p.m. - 10 p.m. was assigned the dinner load. It was not
possible to ﬁnd any useful literature on internal load from cooking. By looking at data
sheets on commercial cookers [65, 66] the energy consumption of cooktops was found to
range from 1200 W to 2200 W and an oven uses on average 2200 W. It was assumed the
typical value for cooking breakfast is 1200 W, 2200 W for lunch and 3600 W for dinner.
The minimum value is set to 0 W and the maximum value is based on maximum power
capacity on cookers which was found from [65, 66] to be approximately 10 kW.
34
==PAGE:59==
4.5. Design parameters and probability density function
Zimmermann et al. gathered data about yearly usage time of TV, laptops and DVD.
The mean yearly usage time of these appliances are 2006 hours for TV, 832 hours for
laptop and 558 hours for DVD. The power of these appliance was weighted with regards
to their usage time in order to get one combined power consumption.
Vacuum cleaners was assumed to be used once per week on a Sunday and based on
experience it takes on average 15 seconds to vacuum clean one square meter ﬂoor area.
The internal load from vacuum cleaning was accounted for in the bedrooms and combined
kitchen and living room and Barthelmes et al. [45] found 10 a.m. to be the time of the
day where the probability of cleaning was highest.
Most tumble dryers are condensing tumble dryers [67] and thus their power consump-
tion will be released to the indoor air and not exhausted outside the building. Palmer et al.
[63] found that tumble dryers on average was running two times a week. In the simulation
model this was modelled to occur each Sunday and Palmer et al. found 9 a.m. as the most
common time people use their tumble dryer. The cycle time for a tumble dryer is set to
1.5 hours based on online test results [68].
Glazing area
The minimum value for glazing area was based on the minimum requirement from Bergsøe
et al. [13] which is 15%. The maximum value was initially based on Vorre et al. [69], but
the value was unrealistic for the apartment building at Nordhavn that has few external
walls in comparison to a detached house. Based on the area of the apartments external
walls a maximum value of 35% was realistic. The typical value is 22% according to [70].
Solar shading factor
The distribution of the solar shading factor was based on Brohus et al. [31] who investi-
gated the inﬂuence of occupants behaviour on the energy consumption of domestic build-
ings. This was done by a sensitivity analysis where the solar shading factor was a design
parameter. The study based the solar shading factor on local building surveys and litera-
ture.
The IDA ICE control macro used for solar shading is based on earlier studies [38] but
was further developed to not only adjust for illuminance but also high indoor operative
temperature. The macro can be seen in the Third Appendix on Figure 34. When the
temperature is above 27◦C or the illuminance is above 2000 lux the solar shading is fully
drawn and when the temperature is below 23◦C and the illuminance is below 1500 lux the
shading is fully open. Additionally it is further extended to function when the apartment
35
==PAGE:60==
4. METHODS
is occupied and not only when the room with the window is occupied. Both parameters
is controlled proportional to make the simulation time shorter.
Night ventilation
In ofﬁce building etc. it is possible to use the AHU during the night and thereby hav-
ing night ventilation. In residences the AHU normally runs the whole day and therefore
window-opening was used as the night ventilation. The distribution of the night ventila-
tion was based on a study by Henriksen and Olesen [47]. From the study it was possible
to derive that approximately 2/5 of the residents always have the bedroom at night for at
least the warm season and some all year around. The typical opening angle is 10 degrees
and the maximum is 75 degrees. From this it was estimated that 60% of apartments have
a night ventilation opening angle of 0 while 20% of apartments have 10 degrees and the
remaining 20% have an angle of 42 degrees.
The night ventilation was initial occurring every night but since the heating system
cannot keep up if the ambient temperature was too low, a lower limit was needed for
ambient temperature where night ventilation was shut off. A parameter variation was
made and the result showed that it is critical for the case apartment when night ventilation
was used while the ambient temperature was below 10◦C. The simulation model was
setup in such a way that it measures the temperature in the evening and if the ambient
temperature was below 10◦C the night ventilation was shut off.
The night ventilation was controlled in the same macro as the general window-opening.
The night ventilation was limited for the three bedroom windows and therefore a new
macro in IDA ICE was made for these windows. Since the normal window-opening func-
tion was deactivated during the night the two signals could be added in the macro. The
IDA ICE macro with night ventilation opening can be seen in the Third Appendix in
Figure 33.
Glazing g-value
The PDF of the glazing g-value are based on Brohus et al.. The study based the distribu-
tion on a local building survey and literature.
36
==PAGE:61==
4.6. Calculation of probability density function
4.6
Calculation of probability density function
When the minimum, typical and maximum value have been determined along with the
most suitable distribution type the probability density function was calculated.
For design parameters assigned a normal-distribution the mean value was the typical
value because the mean and median are the same for a normal distribution. The standard
deviation was calculated as the maximum value of the design parameter subtracted by the
mean value and then divided by three.
For design parameters assigned a log-normal distribution the mean is equal to the
natural logarithm of the typical value. The standard deviation was calculated by ﬁrst us-
ing the excel function LOGNORM.DIST and choosing the maximum value of the design
parameter as the X value and then choosing a guessed value for the standard deviation.
With the solver function in excel the standard deviation could be solved by solving for the
standard deviation that would give a LOGNORM.DIST output of 0.99, because the max-
imum value corresponds to approximately the 0.99th percentile. Two of the distribution
types used in this study can be seen in Figure 4.8 that shows the normal distribution of the
internal load from vacuum cleaning and ﬁgure 4.9 showing the log-normal distribution of
the internal load from fridge-freezer.
Figure 4.8: Normal distribution of internal load from vacuum cleaning.
37
==PAGE:62==
4. METHODS
Figure 4.9: Log-normal distribution of internal load from fridge-freezer.
38
==PAGE:63==
4.7. Sensitivity analysis
4.7
Sensitivity analysis
The global sensitivity analysis was based on Morris method [71] and the method of "El-
ementary Effect" [71, 72, 73] have been selected as the method to perform the global
sensitivity analysis.
The ﬁrst step in the global sensitivity analysis was to assign a probability density
function (PDF) to each design parameter which is described for each of the ten design pa-
rameters in Section 4.5. Based on the distribution of the minimum, typical and maximum
value in relation to each other the most suitable probability density function was chosen
which was either the log-normal distribution or the normal distribution. The mean and
standard deviation were then calculated for the given probability density function. With
the calculated mean and standard deviation the 5th, 25th, 50th, 75th and 95th percentile were
calculated and for simplicity the 5th percentile is called level 1, 25th percentile is called
level 2 and so forth up to level 5. By using percentiles the distance between each level
is equal for all design parameters (relatively speaking) and this is important because it
makes it possible to compare the design parameters on equal terms.
Figure 4.10 shows an example of how the probability density functions are converted
into ﬁve levels (percentiles).
Figure 4.10: Example of how the probability density functions are converted into ﬁve
levels (not the actual values used in this study). (Graphs from [28])
39
==PAGE:64==
4. METHODS
Table 4.10 shows the results from converting the probability density functions into
ﬁve levels for each of the design parameter investigated in this study. As seen in Table
4.10 there are more than ten parameters because the design parameters occupancy load,
window-opening, moisture load and internal load consist of several parts. Overall the ten
main design parameters are still what is being investigated.
Table 4.10: All design parameter and the value for each of the ﬁve levels.
ML: Moisture load, IL: Internal load, KT: Kitchen, LR: Living room, T: Toilet, BR:
Bedroom. The number after T and BR refers to the room numbers and follows the same
order as in ﬁgure 4.1.
The second step was to create an initial randomised vector, consisting of randomly
chosen levels of each design parameter in an order which was also randomised. Figure
4.11 is an example of an initial input vector marked by the the green cells (levels) for each
40
==PAGE:65==
4.7. Sensitivity analysis
design parameter.
Figure 4.11: Example of initial input vector based on randomisation (not actual values
used in the study).
The third step was to change each of the design parameters one at a time in a ran-
domised order and for each change the new vector with its unique combination of levels
for design parameter where used to create the inputs for an IDA ICE model. This process
results in 11 unique vectors and thus 11 IDA ICE models per ventilation control strategy.
The only difference between each new vector is that a level in one design parameters has
been changed. This makes it possible to track what impact the speciﬁc level change has
on the results from the simulation model. An example of the ﬁrst level change from the
initial input vector from Figure 4.11 can be seen in Figure 4.12.
Figure 4.12: Example of stepwise level change from the initial input vector (not actual
values used in the study).
The fourth step was to make sure the variation of all design parameters were reason-
able covered and thus the process of creating an initial input vector and then creating
41
==PAGE:66==
4. METHODS
additional 10 unique vectors was repeated ten times. Ten times is according to [31] the
recommended amount in order to obtain reliable results. The total number of models per
ventilation control strategy thus becomes r(k + 1), r being the number of repetitions and
k the number of parameters. With ten design parameters investigated for each ventilation
control strategy the amount of models is 110 simulation models. For ten different venti-
lation control strategies the total amount of models thus becomes 1100 simulation models.
Figure 4.13 shows all 1100 simulation models. The green cells are the levels which
constitute the initial input vector and the yellow cells are the new level changes that are
changed one at a time for a randomised design parameter. The randomised sequence the
design parameters are picked according to can be seen as numbers to the left of each
design parameter. As an example, the ﬁrst design parameter to be changed in model 1
is moisture load and it is changed from level 4 to level 2. The next design parameter is
occupancy schedule and it is changed from level 3 to level 2 and so forth for all design
parameters.

4. METHODS
There are different methods which can be used when estimating the inﬂuence of each
design parameter and how sensitive or robust the design parameter is. In this study the
Elementary Effects method (EE) was used and it can be calculated from equation 4.9.
EE(x1, ..., xk) = y(x1, x2, ..., xi−1, xi + ∆, xi+1, ..., xk) −y(x1, ..., xk)
∆
(4.9)
Where y(x1, ..., xk) is the result of the model before the design parameter is changed, xi
is the level of the design parameter and ∆is the level change of the design parameter.
When evaluating a control strategy of 110 models, a total of ten EE values for each
design parameter is calculated and therefore 100 EE values in total. When evaluating the
model a total of 400 EE values is calculated since 100 EE values are calculated both for
primary energy consumption, degree hours, RH hours and CO2 hours. The simulation
models sensitivity to each design parameters can then be quantiﬁed by the mean value
(µ) calculated from the corresponding EE values. µ can be calculated with equation 4.10.
µ =
