|EEi|/r
(4.10)
Where EEi is all the EE values calculated for the design parameter in question and r is
the number of EE values calculated.
The standard deviation (σ) is used to evaluate how dependent each design parameter
is in relation to the setup of the rest of the design parameters investigated. The standard
deviation can be calculated with equation 4.11.

4.8. Optimisation of ventilation system
4.8
Optimisation of ventilation system
A selection of input values and IDA ICE components like the AHU, VHR and the control
strategies were optimised as a preliminary study. The control strategy VAV - central RH
(p) was used through the whole preliminary study and optimisation since it is a simple
control strategy and RH sensors are the most common in Denmark [10]. It was assumed
that most of the setup and values found in the optimisation could be applied to the other
control strategies. The design parameters used for the model setup were the level 3 values
from the sensitivity analysis which also is referred to as the typical design parameter
setup.
Optimisation of ventilation control strategies
Guglietti and Pesavento made a macro1 in the simulation model that balanced the supply
and exhaust for the VAV control strategies with a central sensor in the exhaust duct. The
macro made the simulation model slow to simulate and it was not accurate in balancing
the supply and exhaust ventilation. These issues were solved in another macro developed
by Dantas [74]. Dantas used a more simple and effective way compared to Guglietti
and Pesavento. In general Guglietti and Pesavento used a complex weighting method to
create an individual signal representing the airﬂow for each zone and then sending it to
the ventilation system, weighting both for room size and type. Dantas used a method that
predeﬁned an overall balanced minimum and maximum airﬂow for each individual zone,
which made it possible to send a general control signal to all the zones. This setup can
also be seen in the supervisory control used in the IDA ICE software on Figure 4.14. It
is the supervisory control for the proportional (p) central CO2 controlled system (VAV -
Central CO2 (p)). The VAV - Central RH (p) is similar except the sensor input from the
sensor in the return air duct is changed from a CO2 sensor to a RH sensor. The setup
for the stepwise controller (s) is similar to the supervisory control on Figure 4.14 where
the controller is changed from the proportional controller to a stepwise controller. The
setpoint used for the different control strategies can be seen in Table 4.4.
The minimum and maximum airﬂow for each zone can be seen on Table 4.11 where
it also can be seen that the AHU delivers 28.9 l/s when the control signal is 0 and 100.6
l/s when the control signal is 1. The delivered airﬂow from the AHU for control signal
between 0-1 is a linear interpolation between 28.9 l/s and 100.6 l/s.
It was tested if the supervisory control based on the macro designed by Dantas cor-
rectly balance the ventilation system. Figure 4.15 shows a representative day of the year
and it can be seen that the supply and exhaust air ﬂows are balanced. It can be seen that
1A macro is a method in the IDA ICE software to create new ways of processing data in the software
which can be used to a lot of things e.g to create new control strategies

during minimum air ﬂows there is a bit more supply than return air ﬂows. The difference
between the supply and return air ﬂows might be because of the temperature difference
between the supply and exhaust making the supply air expand when heated in the apart-
ment which then makes the volume of air extracted larger than the volume supplied. The
difference between supply and return airﬂow is largest in the heating season and lowest
during the summer period which supports the proposed explanation. Over time there will
be a balance in the mass of the air going in and the air going out of the apartment.
The supervisory control for the systems with central temperature sensor is different
from the systems with CO2 and RH sensors, since the design parameter setpoint for space
heating can interfere during the winter in the cases where the setpoint is above 23◦C.

Therefore the temperature setpoint in the ventilation control for the system with temper-
ature sensor is different during the winter where the ventilation setpoint was changed to
25.5◦C. This setup can also be seen in the Third Appendix in Figure 30.
The only ventilation control strategy where the supervisory control is not used is the
CAV system. For all other control strategies it is necessary to use supervisory control to
balance the return and supply airﬂow. For the three control strategies with local sensors it
is common to setup the control locally in each zone. Since each zone is unbalanced with
regards to return and supply airﬂow the whole ventilation system will be unbalanced if
the setup of the control is locally. The setup for the two control strategies where all three
types of sensors are used can be seen in Figure 4.16. The last control strategy using only
room based RH sensors is a simple version of the one seen in Figure 4.16 where only the
controller for RH is used. The supervisory control for this control strategy is shown in
the Third Appendix in Figure 31.
For all the controls where the supervisory control is used the zone control is built up as
shown in the Third Appendix in Figure 32. It is a simple setup where it links the general
control signal given from the supervisory control into each zone’ ventilation signal.
The CAV system is setup using the default zone control for CAV in IDA ICE and
where the airﬂow to each zone is adjusted to what is needed to meet the requirements
in the building regulation of 0.3 l/s per m2 heated ﬂoor area, 15 l/s in bathrooms and 20
l/s in the kitchen. Since the airﬂows were not boosted in the simulation models with for

Ventilation heat recovery optimisation
The VHR is by default in the IDA ICE software controlled by the setpoint of the supply air
temperature which both can be constant over the year, schedule based or controlled after
48
==PAGE:73==
4.8. Optimisation of ventilation system
the ambient temperature. During the heating season, energy can be saved by maximising
the VHR efﬁciency but the supply temperature should not exceed the indoor temperature
setpoint. In the simulation models a constant supply temperature of 20◦C seems reason-
able during the heating season. During the summer period there is a risk of overheating
and while the ambient temperature is high enough to be supplied directly into the zones
it will bypass the VHR in order to avoid reheating of the zones. To avoid draught the air
supply temperature should not be below 18 ◦C and preferably not below 20 ◦C [75]. The
difference in energy use and indoor climate when using 18 ◦C, 19 ◦C or 20 ◦C as sup-
ply air temperature during the summer period was preliminary investigated with the IDA
ICE software. The investigation combined two different VHR control methods, ambient
temperature control and schedule based control in the typical model setup from the sen-
sitivity analysis with VAV - central RH (p). This resulted in ﬁve different combinations
which is schedule based VHR control with a constant air supply temperature of 20◦C
(C20) all year. Ambient temperature VHR control with either an air supply temperature
of 18◦C (Te18) or 19◦C (Te19) during periods with ambient temperature above 13◦C.
Schedule based VHR control with either 18◦C (S18) or 19◦C (S19) during the summer
period deﬁned as the ﬁve month outside the heating season. The results can be seen in
Table 4.13.
Table 4.13: The evaluation of VHR control method. Evaluated for the VAV controlled

The overall evaluation of the best control of the VHR is based on the Energy & indoor
49
==PAGE:74==
4. METHODS
climate weighted value to the average (EI) which is shown in equation 4.8. The lowest
EI value for the models is the best control method when assuming the three combined
indoor climate parameter is of equal importance as the primary energy consumption. The
constant temperature control of 20◦C for the VHR is both the best in matter of EI, draught
is low due to the higher temperature and it is also the simplest control method. The
energy consumption for C20 is in average 1% lower than the other VHR controls but it is
especially RH that makes the difference. It got in average 20% less amount of RH hours
compared to the other VHR controls. The constant temperature control for the VHR of
20 ◦C is therefore used for all the ventilation control strategies investigated.
Relative humidity setpoint optimisation
Initial simulations of models with humidity control revealed that the setpoint for humidity
control has a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the primary energy consumption. One reason being
that in the summertime ambient moisture content for DRY weather-data can be high. If
there is no setback to the minimum ventilation rates when the ambient moisture content
is above the interior moisture content and above the RH limit the ventilation system will
run on full power. A setup of the setback control for too high exterior moisture content in
IDA ICE supervisory control is shown in Figure 4.17.
Figure 4.17: Setback control to minimum airﬂow when the exterior moisture content
exceeds the interior moisture content.
There is also an issue of where to set the lower and the higher proportional band (p-
band) for the humidity control. If the lower p-band is low it prevents the moisture building
up in the apartment but it also use more energy. If a high p-band is used the control reacts
slower when there is moisture production resulting in more hours with too high RH. The
characteristics used for local humidity control by Guglietti and Pesavento can be seen in
50
==PAGE:75==
4.8. Optimisation of ventilation system
ﬁgure 4.18. Another study by Bergsøe and Afsari [76] investigated humidity controlled
housing ventilation and their characteristics used for local humidity control can be seen
in ﬁgure 4.19.
Figure 4.18: Characteristics used for local humidity control by Guglietti and Pesavento
[11]
Figure 4.19: Characteristics used for local humidity control by Bergsøe and Afsari [76]
A parameter investigation was made where the minimum and maximum values for
the lower and higher p-band were deﬁned based on what the two above mentioned studies
used. For the lower p-band a maximum value of 50% and a minimum value of 25% was
used and for the higher p-band a maximum value of 80% and a minimum value of 55%
was used. The investigation was setup for steps between minimum and maximum of 5%-
points resulting in a total of 36 scenarios which again was investigated with and without
exterior moisture content setback. All the results from the 72 runs and the Energy &
indoor climate weighed values (EI) can be seen in the Second Appendix. A lower p-band
of 25% and a higher p-band of 70% is the most optimal control for both the system with
and without ambient moisture content setback when evaluated for the EI value. Both of
51
==PAGE:76==
4. METHODS
the controls have an average EI value which is 5.7% lower than the other controls with
and without setback. The control with and without setback are nearly equally good but
the control with the setback performs slightly better. The control with setback has 1.5%
lower energy consumption and 7.4% lower amount of RH hours but got 4.5% more degree
hours and 5% more CO2 hours than the control without setback. Since the control with
setback needs more sensors and control it is more expensive and therefore the control
without setback and a lower p-band of 25% and a higher p-band of 70% is used.

5. RESULTS
5.1
Ventilation control strategies with typical design
parameters
When evaluating the ten ventilation control strategies simulated with typical design pa-
rameters, a clear relation between energy use and indoor climate can be observed in Figure
5.1. Generally more energy use equals better indoor climate for all the ventilation control
strategies. The ventilation control strategy with local RH sensors stands out compared to
the other control strategies by providing a good indoor climate while having a low pri-
mary energy consumption. The control strategy with central RH(p) sensors also deviate
from the trend line with a low-energy consumption and a mediocre indoor climate. The
ventilation control strategies with the best indoor climate are those using the local sensors
with all sensor types. The ventilation control strategies with a reduced minimum venti-
lation rate of 0.1 l/s per m2 heated ﬂoor area has a lower energy consumption than the
control strategy with a minimum ventilation rate of 0.3 l/s per m2 heated ﬂoor area and
this is possible without affecting the indoor climate. The ventilation control strategy with
the worst indoor climate are the ones with central CO2 sensors. They have approximately
80% more hours above the limit for the three indoor climate evaluation parameters than
the average of the ten control strategies, where VAV - All (p), min and VAV - All (p) have
60% less hours above the limit than the average control strategy.
Using a stepwise controlled system compared to a proportional controlled system has
a very low inﬂuence on the results for the strategy with central CO2 sensors. The results
are almost identical which is why they share the same point on Figure 5.1. The differ-
ence between using a stepwise system instead of a proportional system also has minor
inﬂuence on the ventilation control strategies with central temperature sensor. Using a
stepwise controlled system instead of the proportional has a larger inﬂuence on the ven-
tilation control strategy with central RH sensor. The one controlled proportionally has a
lower primary energy consumption than the stepwise system, but the indoor climate are
also worse for the proportional controlled system. The strategy using CAV has the third
best indoor climate but also the third highest primary energy consumption.

As described earlier the EI value is the average of the weighted indoor climate pa-
rameters CO2 hours, temperature hours and RH hours which then is being averaged with
the weighted primary energy consumption. A large EI value equals bad performance on
either indoor climate, high energy consumption or a combination of the two. Figure 5.2
compares the ventilation control strategies on their EI score and also shows the weighted
values of each evaluation parameter. When evaluated on the EI score the ventilation con-
trol strategies performing the best are the strategies with local sensors, where VAV - All
(p), min performs slightly better than the other two systems with local sensors. The venti-
lation control strategy with CAV performs better than all the ventilation control strategies
with a sensor in the exhaust duct. VAV - All (p), min and VAV - All (p) are the only systems
able to provide zero CO2 hours. The ventilation control strategies using a central sensor
in the exhaust duct have the worst performance because of a poor indoor climate, where
the systems controlled by RH and T manage to keep RH hours and temperature hours
low. VAV - Central RH (s) performs the best of all the ventilation control strategies with
central sensor in the exhaust duct.

==PAGE:82==
5. RESULTS
Degree hours, CO2 hours and RH hours are not commonly used as an evaluation
parameter, hence it can be difﬁcult to relate to the values. Another way of evaluation
the three indoor climate parameters is with a duration curve and that can be seen for all
strategies and indoor climate evaluation parameters in Figure 5.3, 5.4 and 5.5. The X-axis
is zoomed in to make it possible to see the difference between the strategies when the limit
for indoor climate parameters are exceeded. There is a difference between the calculated
hours and the duration curves where the duration curves include hours when the rooms
are not occupied where it is neglected when calculating degree hours and CO2 hours.
Duration curves without the X-axis are zoomed in and for the entire duration period can
be seen in the Fifth Appendix.
In Figure 5.3 it can be seen that the operative temperature is above 27◦C at least 7%
of the time for all control strategies even for the strategies with local sensors. For all ten
control strategies the temperature reaches above 30◦C at some point during the year. The
two control strategies with all local sensor types manages to control the temperature the
best, while the strategies with central CO2 sensor and the VAV - Central RH (p) strategy
have the highest temperature. The two CO2 controlled systems do not comply with the
27◦C limit 22% of the time. The CAV system, VAV - Central RH (s) and both the central
temperature controlled systems have a mediocre performance where the system controlled
by temperature stands out with lower temperature at the worst case scenario.
The worst control strategies with regards to CO2 concentration are the two central CO2
controlled systems which can be seen on Figure 5.4. The proportional controlled system
has 5.5% of the time where it is above 900 ppm and the stepwise controlled system has
7% of the time where it is above the limit. The VAV - Central RH(p) and VAV - RH (p)
have a mediocre performance and exceed the limit approximately 4%-5% of the time.
The ventilation system with CAV manages to meet the limit of 900 ppm for 98% of
the time and has a maximum concentration of around 1000 ppm. The best performing
control strategies are the control strategies with all sensor types and both of them meet
the requirements for CO2 concentration for the entire year and reaches 870 ppm in the
worst case.
As seen on Figure 5.5 the X-axis only spans from 95%-100%. None of the control
strategies comply with the requirement of 60% the whole year but the VAV - RH(p), CAV
and VAV - Central RH (s) comply with the limit for more than 99% of the time. The only
control strategy which has more than 1.5% of the time above 60% is the VAV - Central
CO2 (p) and it does not comply with the limit for 2.2% of the time.

5. RESULTS
5.2
Overall sensitivity investigation
110 IDA ICE models have been simulated for each ventilation control strategy which
correspond to 1100 simulation models in total. The results are summarised into 110
primary energy consumption values for each ventilation control strategy and these are
presented as a boxplot for each control strategy on Figure 5.6. The same has been done
for the operative temperature results shown in Figure 5.7, CO2 concentration in Figure 5.8
and RH in Figure 5.9. A reference line is inserted for degree hours, CO2 hours and RH
hours to make it possible to relate the results to a scenario. The reference line for degree
hours is for a scenario where the four occupied rooms are 3◦C above the limit for 5% of
the year which is equal to 5256 degree hours. For the CO2 hours the reference scenario is
where the four occupied rooms have a CO2 concentration 200 ppm above the limit for 5%
of a year resulting in 350,400 CO2 hours. The reference scenario for RH hours is where
5% of the year the RH are 5% above the limit for the whole apartment which is equal to
2190 RH hours.
The boxplots for primary energy consumption in Figure 5.6 shows that the median
of the two control strategies with central CO2 sensor have the lowest primary energy
consumption of all the control strategies. The median of VAV- All (p) is the highest of
all the control strategies. The strategy with CAV has a median with a mediocre primary
energy consumption when compared to the other strategies. The range of values and
interquantile range shows how robust each strategy are, how well they respond to new
conditions created by the parameter variations. VAV - All (p) and VAV - All (p), min have
the shortest interquantile range of all the strategies. VAV - Central T (s) has the shortest
range when all data points are considered. CAV has the largest range and the highest
primary energy consumption in worst case scenario of all the control strategies.
Figure 5.7 shows how the control strategies perform in regards to degree hours. The
two strategies with central CO2 sensor have the highest median and range of degree hours.
VAV - All (p) and VAV - All (p), min have the lowest median and range of degree hours.
The two VAV - Central T strategies also have a low median and range of degree hours.
The boxplots of each strategy when evaluated on CO2 hours is shown in Figure 5.8.
The two strategies with central CO2 sensor have the highest median and range of CO2
hours. CAV, VAV - All (p), min and VAV - All (p) have very low median and range of CO2
hours compared to the other strategies.
Figure 5.9 shows the boxplots on RH hours for each strategy. All the control strategies
with central sensor in the exhaust duct have high median RH hours and range of values,
except for the strategies with a central RH sensor. The VAV - RH (p) strategy has one of
the lowest median RH hours and the narrowest range of RH hours followed by CAV, VAV
- All (p) and VAV - All (p), min.

5.3. Sensitivity analysis of design parameters
5.3
Sensitivity analysis of design parameters
The inﬂuence of the ten design parameters on the evaluation parameters are ranked ac-
cording to their inﬂuence and this is presented as horizontal bar charts. Each of the ten
design parameters are ranked four times for each control strategy. One showing how
sensitive the primary energy consumption of the control strategy is to change of each de-
sign parameter. Another showing the same but for the operative temperature, the third
showing it for the CO2 concentration and the last is for the RH. The inﬂuence of the de-
sign parameters is calculated according to the Elementary Effect Method as described in
chapter 4.7 by the use of equation 4.10 that calculate the mean (µ). The colour of each
horizontal bar in the ﬁgures indicate how dependent the inﬂuence of the design parameter
is to the other design parameters. This is also calculated according to the Elementary
Effect Method as described in chapter 4.7 by the use of equation 4.11 that calculate the
standard deviation (σ). The red colour means high dependency, yellow means medium
dependency and green colour means low dependency. In each ﬁgure it is also shown how
each design parameter inﬂuences the evaluation parameter by arrows inserted before the
name of each design parameter. If a higher level of the design parameter has a tendency
to be proportional with the evaluated parameter the following arrow is used ↑, if it is in-
versely proportional this arrow is used ↓or if it is a combination the following arrow is
used ↕.
The design parameter occupancy schedule has deliberately been removed as an inves-
tigated parameter in the sensitivity analysis of the degree hours and CO2 hours. This is
because the degree hours and CO2 hours are only counted when the zone are occupied as
described in chapter 4.2. The occupancy schedule inﬂuence how many and for how long
rooms are occupied resulting in a direct inﬂuence on the results for degree hours and CO2
hours and due to this dependency it has been removed from the results.
The results for three chosen control strategies are shown on the following pages. The
selection of the strategy is based on covering all types of strategies, so the CAV, VAV -
central RH(p) and VAV - All (P), min are selected to cover strategies with/without VAV
and with/without local sensors.

==PAGE:92==
5. RESULTS
Figure 5.10 to 5.13 show the results from the control strategy with CAV. The primary
energy consumption is most inﬂuenced by the setpoint for space heating, see Figure 5.10.
The primary energy consumption increases with more than 4 kWh/m2/year for each level
the setpoint for space heating is increased. window-opening has the second largest inﬂu-
ence followed by glazing size, internal load, occupancy schedule and night ventilation but
the primary energy consumption is changed with less than 1.5 kWh/m2/year for each level
change of these design parameters. The last design parameters only change the primary
energy consumption with less than 0.25 kWh/m2/year. The highlighted design parameters
with the exception of internal load and occupancy schedule have low standard deviation
as indicated by the green colour and thus they are not very dependent on one or more
of the other design parameters. Internal load and occupancy schedule are to some extent
dependent on one or more of the other parameters as indicated by the orange colour.
Degree hours are by far most inﬂuenced by window-opening as seen in Figure 5.11.
Degree hours decreases with more than 6000 ◦Ch for each level the window-opening is
increased. The inﬂuence of window-opening is not very dependent on one or more of the
other parameters as indicated by the light green colour. Higher level of night ventilation
decreases the degree hours with 1600 ◦Ch for each level change and for the remaining
parameters the change is less than approximately 1000 ◦Ch for each level change.
CO2 hours are to some extend only inﬂuenced by occupancy load where the remaining
parameters have none to minor inﬂuence. See Figure 5.12. Occupancy load are to some
extend dependent on one or more of the other parameters as indicated by the yellow
colour. CO2 hours increases with 70,000 ppmh for each level of occupancy load which is
increased.
RH hours are affected by a lot of the design parameters, but window-opening, mois-
ture load, night ventilation and occupancy schedule are the four design parameters with
the largest inﬂuence. See Figure 5.13. These parameters inﬂuence on RH hours range
between 300-500%h per level change and all of the them are proportional with RH hours.
Only night ventilation is to some extent dependent on the other design parameters as
indicated by the orange colour.

5.3. Sensitivity analysis of design parameters
Table 5.3 shows the overall ranking of the design parameters according to their in-
ﬂuence on the primary energy consumption, degree hours, CO2 hours and RH hours. It
is based on a summation of the design parameter ranking for each control strategy in
order to make a general ranking. The general ranking does not necessarily reﬂect the de-
sign parameter ranking of each control strategy. A low score means high inﬂuence on the
evaluation parameters and a high score means low inﬂuence on the evaluation parameters.
This method of ranking the parameters’ inﬂuence does not reﬂect the size of difference
between each score and the difference between the second and most inﬂuential design
parameter can be very large.
Each design parameters inﬂuence on the evaluation parameter is also summarised
where 1 means proportional, 0 is a combination and -1 is inversely proportional to the
evaluated parameter. The column in the table shows the average of the values for all ten
strategies. It is assumed that an average of 0.35 to 1 shows that a higher level of the design
parameter has a tendency to be proportional (↑), -1 to -0.35 is assumed to have a tendency
of inversely proportional (↓) and if the average is between -0.35 to 0.35 then an increase
in the level of the parameter is assumed to be a combination (↕).
Setpoint for space heating has the highest inﬂuence overall on the primary energy
consumption for the strategies investigated, followed by window-opening and glazing
size. The solar shading factor has the lowest inﬂuence followed by glazing g-value and
occupancy load.
Window-opening is for all strategies the design parameter with the highest inﬂuence
on degree hours followed by night ventilation and internal load. Moisture load, setpoint
for space heating and occupancy load have the smallest inﬂuence.
Window-opening is the design parameter with the most inﬂuence on RH hours, fol-
lowed by moisture load and night ventilation. Solar shading factor, glazing g-value and
glazing size are the parameters with the smallest inﬂuence.
For most strategies occupancy load is the design parameter with most inﬂuence on
CO2 hours, followed by window-opening and setpoint for space heating. The least inﬂu-
ential design parameters are glazing size, solar shading factor and glazing g-value.

CHAPTER 6
Discussion
It is important to make a clear distinction between energy consumption and indoor climate
when evaluating the ventilation control strategies. Limits have been set for the indoor
climate and the ventilation system is expected to make sure these limits are not exceeded.
There is also a limit for the building energy consumption but the ventilation systems
main objective is to ensure good indoor climate and not necessarily lowering the energy
consumption. There exists several other building technologies capable of lowering the
energy consumption in order to comply with the limit for energy consumption which is
not a part of the objective for the thesis. If a ventilation control strategy does not comply
with the limits for the indoor climate parameters it does not meet the expectations and
the energy consumption is thus secondary. If there are two ventilation control strategies
which both comply with the limits for the indoor climate then the energy consumption is
an important aspect.
In this study the limit for temperature was 27◦C, 900 ppm for CO2 concentration and
60% for RH, which is based on regulations and guidelines. Due to Danish traditions it is
not always possible to comply with the RH and temperature limits. There is no tradition
in Danish residences to use a cooling coil in the AHU and thus it is allowed in BR18 for
the indoor temperature to exceed 27◦C on warm days. As a reference to the warm days
the DRY2013 weather ﬁle has 11 hours above 27◦C for ambient air temperature. Another
topic that should be mentioned when evaluating the temperature control for the ventilation
control strategies is the methods used to prevent overheating in Danish residences often
focus on window and glazing design, solar shading, thermal mass etc. This means sev-
eral other parameters can minimise and inﬂuence the overheating in the apartment which
should be included in the evaluation of the control strategies. BR18 deﬁnes no direct
requirements for CO2 concentration and RH, though RH should not damage the building
due to condensation. There is no tradition for dehumidifying the supply air in Danish
residencies and without dehumidifying the air it is impossible to keep the RH below 60%
79
==PAGE:104==
6. DISCUSSION
when the outdoor moisture content corresponds to a indoor RH of 60% or above. As
a reference the AHU used in the simulations models cannot meet the RH requirements
40 hours a year or 147 RH hours1. CO2 concentration is a parameter that can be fully
controlled by supplying ambient air without any other interventions required since the
CO2 concentration of the ambient air is relative stable. This means the ventilation system
can be expected to keep the CO2 concentration below the speciﬁed limit. The limit for
CO2 concentration in residences is based on the homeowners preferences when the build-
ing is constructed due to no regulatory limits and the limit can thereby vary for different
residences.
6.1
Best performing ventilation control strategy
The main objective was to investigate which control strategy performs the best with re-
gards to primary energy consumption and indoor climate. When evaluated on the EI score
the VAV - All (p), min scored the lowest EI value as seen from Figure 5.2. This means
it has the overall best performance on primary energy consumption and indoor climate
when simulated with typical design parameters. It is also the only strategy including VAV
- All (p) capable of complying all year with the limit for CO2 concentration for both the
model with typical design parameters and nearly all the 110 simulation models from the
sensitivity analysis. VAV - All (p), min has the second highest primary energy consump-
tion which to a large degree is explained by its ability to provide a good indoor climate,
which is derived from the tendency that systems with poor indoor climate also have low
primary energy consumption and vice versa (See Figure 5.1).
VAV - All (p), min is the second best system to avoid overheating and has 351 degree
hours while VAV - All (p) has 349 degree hours which is nearly the same. The same applies
when the design parameters are changed in the sensitivity analysis where VAV - All (p),
min and VAV - All (p) are best to adjust to the new conditions caused by the variation of
the design parameters.
When evaluating the amount of RH hours of the control strategies simulated with
typical design parameters the VAV - All (p), min is outperformed by both CAV that has
approximately 19% fewer RH hours and VAV - RH (p) that has approximately 40% fewer
RH hours. The reason for this is not because VAV - All (p), min is not able to ventilate
enough or to detect the RH level but rather the contrary, which will be discussed in more
detail later. As seen in Figure 5.5 all control strategies except for one comply with the
limit for RH in approximately 99% of the time and it is only during the summer time
the limit is exceeded and the risk of condensation is much more limited this time of year.
1For how it is calculated see the Seventh Appendix
80
==PAGE:105==
6.1. Best performing ventilation control strategy
VAV - All (p), min is also one of the most robust ventilation control strategies for all
four evaluation parameters which also means that the ventilation system for example can
handle a variation in the user behaviour better than most of the other strategies. If it is
important that the apartment to a large degree corresponds to the expected indoor climate
and energy consumption then a robust system should be selected.
VAV - All (p), min has a primary energy consumption of 17 kWh/m2/year when sim-
ulated with typical design parameters. In the sensitivity analysis it has a median of 14
kWh/m2/year and a maximum of 24 kWh/m2/year. The limit for Building Class 2020
is 20 kWh/m2/year and that is including domestic hot water which is not included in the
primary energy consumption calculated for the control strategies. This makes it important
to be aware of other building parameters that can lower the primary energy consumption
when using VAV - All (p), min otherwise it can be a challenge to comply with the energy
requirements for Building Class 2020. What separates VAV - All (p), min from most of
the other control strategies is the software requirements and amount of sensors used and
from an economic perspective this is more costly and more sensitive to malfunction be-
cause more things can go wrong with the system. This might not be an issue but it is still
something to be considered.
With the present BR18 the VAV - All (p), min does not comply with the requirement
of a minimum ventilation rate of 0.3 l/s per m2 heated ﬂoor area. If the requirements are
changed to a lower ventilation rate, then VAV - All (p), min would be an advantageous
ventilation control strategy. VAV - All (p) has a minimum ventilation rate of 0.3 l/s per m2
heated ﬂoor area and that is the only thing separating it from VAV - All (p), min. The larger
ventilation rate makes the primary energy consumption of VAV - All (p) 8%2 higher per
year compared to VAV - All (p), min, but provide the same indoor climate and robustness
against design parameter variations. Pollution sources like Volatile Organic Compounds
(VOC), benzene, formaldehyde etc. are also important to be aware of especially when
low ventilation rates are used as for the case with VAV - All (p), min. This subject is
not covered in this study but an earlier study by Guglietti and Pesavento [11] found that
similar ventilation control strategies was capable of keeping the VOC exposure at an ac-
ceptable level for a reduction period of 10 hours after a run-in period of a month. The
reduction period is a period where the ventilation rate is constant 0.1 l/s per m2 heated
ﬂoor area. Based on this investigation it seems possible to reduce the ventilation rate one
month after the residence has been build and furnished.
If full compliance with the limit of 900 ppm for the CO2 concentration is not necessary
some of the other ventilation control strategies are also good candidates. It could be in
2When simulated with typical design parameters
81
==PAGE:106==
6. DISCUSSION
situations where another limit is used for CO2 concentration or it is acceptable to exceed
the limit for a certain amount of hours in a year.
VAV - RH (p) has the second best score when evaluated on EI as seen in Figure 5.2.
VAV - RH (p) has 248,373 CO2 hours when simulated with typical design parameters.
The CO2 duration curve in Figure 5.4 shows that 4% of the time the CO2 concentration is
above 900 ppm and a little more than 2% of the time it is above 1000 ppm. This means it
will comply if the limits are changed to 1000 ppm and it is acceptable to exceed the limit
3% of the time. When evaluating on the operative temperature it can be seen from Figure
5.3 that it is above 27◦C 15% of the time and above 28◦C 10% of the time which exceeds
the acceptable limits of time above these temperatures. To comply with the thermal in-
door climate while using VAV - RH (p) it requires an optimisation for other parameters
capable of lowering overheating. The system provides indoor air with the lowest level
of RH of all control strategies investigated and 60% RH is exceeded less than 1% of the
time. It has a primary energy consumption of 13 kWh/m2/year3, which is 31 % lower than
VAV - All (p) and it would therefore be easier to comply with the energy requirements.
As seen from Figure 5.6 to 5.9 it has a below average robustness with regards to primary
energy consumption, mediocre robustness with regards to degree hours, CO2 hours and
the best robustness with regards to RH hours. The system will therefore function well in
environment where the moisture load varies a lot. On the other hand it can be difﬁcult to
predict the energy consumption since its energy consumption is very sensitive to param-
eter variation.
The CAV control strategy is the most simple control strategy of the ones investigated
but scores well on the EI value, better than the control strategies with a central sensor. It
provides an indoor climate with 23,844 CO2 hours which translates into less than 2.5% of
the time with a CO2 concentration above 900 ppm and almost never above 1000 ppm (see
Figure 5.4). This complies with the limit of 900 ppm if it is allowed to exceed the limits
for 3% of the time. It is the third best system to control the CO2 concentration. The oper-
ative temperature is above 27◦C for 13% of the time and above 28◦C for 8% of the time,
with a total of 915 degree hours. Even though it is the third best system to control the
temperature it is still far from complying with the requirements for thermal comfort. The
apartment needs to to have other installations capable of lowering overheating in order to
comply with the requirements for overheating. The CAV control strategy provides indoor
air that is above 60% RH less than 1% of the time as seen in Figure 5.5 and is among the
best systems to avoid too high RH. Despite the system cannot change the ventilation rate it
is still a robust system against design parameter variations when evaluated for the indoor
3Excluding domestic hot water
82
==PAGE:107==
6.2. Further evaluation of ventilation control strategies
climate when compared to some of the other ventilation control strategies with a central
sensor (see Figure 5.7 to 5.9). On the other hand it is the most sensitive system with
regards to primary energy consumption where the maximum value is 29 kWh/m2/year3,
the upper quartile is 17.5 kWh/m2/year3 and the median is 11.5 kWh/m2/year3 when eval-
uated in the sensitivity analysis. In the case with typical design parameters the primary
energy consumption is 16 kWh/m2/year3. It can be an issue that the apartment can have a
much higher energy consumption than expected when taken into use, otherwise the CAV
system preforms well.
6.2
Further evaluation of ventilation control strategies
This section evaluates each ventilation control strategy further and tries to explain why
they performed as they do.
The control strategies with central CO2 sensor should in principle have zero CO2
hours because they are controlled after the CO2 concentration. The reality is quite the op-
posite as the two systems have the highest CO2 concentration of all the control strategies
investigated. Further analysis foundnd the reason to be the positioning of the CO2 sensor,
which is placed in the exhaust duct. The air measured by the CO2 sensor is a mixture of
all the air taken from the rooms with exhaust units, which are the two bathrooms and the
combined living room and kitchen. Figure 6.1 shows the CO2 concentration in bedroom
1, bathroom 1 and the living room over the period of a day in the model using the venti-
lation control strategy with central CO2 sensor (p). It is observed that the concentration
reaches above 1400 ppm in bedroom 1 at 6 a.m. after a steady increase during the night.
Meanwhile the concentration in the living room is 470 ppm and in the bathroom 750 ppm,
substantially lower than in bedroom 1. The resulting CO2 concentration in the exhaust
duct are 550 ppm which is below the lower band of 800 ppm and the control signal to the
AHU is therefore zero which makes it runs on minimum ventilation rate.
This inability to detect critical indoor air quality levels in rooms explains why the
ventilation control strategies with a central sensor in the exhaust duct have difﬁculties to
even control for the measured and controlled parameter. Critical RH and air temperature
levels in a single room are in a similar way diluted when mixed in the exhaust duct with
the air from the other rooms.
CO2 concentration is the worst parameter to control for when using a central sensor
in the exhaust duct since the CO2 concentration often is high in smaller fractions of the
apartment while it can be low in the rest as shown in the example. The systems with RH
sensor or temperature sensor are not inﬂuenced only by one source as the system with CO2
sensor. Multiple sources can increase the RH or temperature and some of these sources
83
==PAGE:108==
6. DISCUSSION
Figure 6.1: CO2 concentration in bedroom 1, bathroom 1, living room and the concentra-
tion measured by the central CO2 sensor and the resulting control output. Shown for the
16th of May in the simulation model for VAV - Central CO2 (p).
are the ambient conditions like solar radiation, outdoor temperature or moisture content
in the air. These sources often affect multiple rooms simultaneously and therefore RH
and temperature rise more equally in the whole apartment than the CO2 concentration
does. Temperature and especially RH have most of their sources in rooms where the
air is extracted which also improves their ability to be controlled centrally. This can
also be seen in Figure 6.1 where the central sensor signal increases more when the CO2
concentration in the bathroom is increased than for the bedroom even though the bedroom
is the largest of the two rooms and has a higher concentration. The RH and temperature
are therefore more suitable as a control parameter than CO2, when used in the strategy
with a central sensor.
The ventilation system with central RH sensor performs the best of all the ventila-
tion systems with a central sensor and the reason could be the wide range between the
low band of 25% and the high band of 70% in the controller. This makes the ventilation
system react to low concentrations which diminish the effect of air mixing in the exhaust
duct. The range is relatively narrow for the temperature controlled ventilation control
strategies with a low band of 23◦C and high band of 27◦C and even narrower for the CO2
controlled with a low band of 800 ppm and a high band of 900 ppm which could explain
84
==PAGE:109==
6.2. Further evaluation of ventilation control strategies
the difference in performance. Another reason could be that the moisture is polluting
more than CO2 which was the case in the dimensioning of the AHU from Chapter 4.1 and
this combined with the fact that the pollution of moisture often happens in the smaller
internal bathrooms where the air is extracted and windows cannot be opened increases
the ventilation rate compared to other central controlled strategies.
When comparing the RH hours for the three ventilation control strategies with local
sensors the VAV - RH (p) has the lowest amount of RH hours. The two other control
strategies also have a RH sensor placed in the same rooms, thus they should in principle
have the same number of RH hours. One of the reason why they do not have the same RH
hours is because when the CO2 sensor or temperature sensor signals the AHU to ventilate
it is regardless of whether or not the moisture content is larger outside than inside which
can cause the RH to rise in the apartment. The indoor air temperature is also lower than
for the VAV - RH (p) which inﬂuences the RH to increase and thereby the amount of RH
hours.
All the investigated control strategies except for VAV - Central CO2 (p) exceed 60%
relative humidity less than 1.5% of the time, which means too high relative humidity is
seldom an issue.
When investigating the performance between a stepwise controlled system and a pro-
portional controlled system there is almost no difference in performance for the ventila-
tion control strategies with a central CO2 sensor. The difference for the central temper-
ature controlled is also minor but for the central RH controlled the difference between
stepwise and proportional controlled is more striking. The reason for this probably has
something to do with the range between the low and high controller band for the different
control strategies or how equally the controlled parameter is distributed over this range
when looking at a whole year. For the strategy with central RH sensor the proportional
controlled system gradually increases the ventilation rate while the stepwise spikes up to
50% ventilation rate when the RH reaches the low band of 25% and then another spike
occurs up to full ventilation capacity when the high band is reached at 70%. This makes
the stepwise controlled system run at a higher ventilation rate when the RH is between
25% and 47.5% than for the proportionally controlled but it also has lower rates when it
is between 47.5% and 70%. As seen in Figure 6.2 the RH has more hours in the year
below 47.5% than above and is below 47.5% for most of the heating season and thus the
stepwise controlled system will ventilate more than the proportionally controlled result-
ing in higher energy consumption and better indoor climate. The CO2 and temperature
sensor based systems have a narrower range between the low and high control band which
can explain why the difference between stepwise and proportional control systems is very
small for these strategies. The CO2 concentration in the exhaust duct is rarely above the
85
==PAGE:110==
6. DISCUSSION
low band of 800 ppm resulting in a nearly constant ventilation rate of 0.3 l/s/m2 for both
of the central CO2 controlled systems.
Figure 6.2: The measured RH in the exhaust duct for the VAV central RH(p) during the
whole year of 2010.
6.3
Ventilation control strategies and the sensitivity
analysis
The sensitivity analysis can be used for more than evaluating how adaptable each ventila-
tion control strategy is to new design parameter conditions, it can also be used to evaluate
the inﬂuence of each design parameter. This knowledge can be used to optimise the con-
ditions for ventilation system to perform the best when designing new buildings. It can
also be used to improve current ventilation systems which do not perform as intended and
where a redesign of the ventilation system is not an option.
For all the investigated ventilation control strategies window-opening inﬂuences the
operative temperature the most, followed by night ventilation and then internal load. If
high temperature is a problem in residencies installed with one of the investigated ventila-
tion systems then the ﬁrst thing to improve should be how window-opening is controlled.
If manually then the residents should open the windows more often and if it is automati-
cally controlled then the control should be changed.
There is no clear pattern among the ventilation control strategies on which design pa-
rameter is the most inﬂuential on CO2 concentration, RH and primary energy consump-
86
==PAGE:111==
6.3. Ventilation control strategies and the sensitivity analysis
tion. For most systems primary energy consumption is by far most inﬂuenced by the
setpoint for space heating, followed by window-opening and glazing size. A higher level
for the setpoint for space heating, window-opening and glazing size are all proportional
with primary energy consumption. If some issues occur with high energy consumption or
there is a desire to save energy the setpoint for space heating is a good place to start. In
general it should be well-considered what setpoint for space heating is used.
The reason glazing size is proportional with primary energy consumption can be be-
cause the window-opening area increases when the glazing size increases and this over-
rules the internal heat gain from solar radiation through the window. This might not
reﬂect reality because it is assumed all types of window sizes open the same way, but
in reality there are differences. Large windows in contrast to small windows often have
a section of the window that can be opened and not the entire window. Whether or not
glazing size is proportional with primary energy consumption is thus questionable. As
a comparison the g-value and glazing size in this investigation does not have the same
proportionality to the energy consumption which could be expected due to both of them
increasing the solar gain. It should also be mentioned that glazing size also causes higher
thermal transmittance through the envelope unlike the g-value which also play a role.
For most of the ventilation control strategies the amount of RH hours is inﬂuenced
the most by window-opening, followed by moisture load and night ventilation. For both
window-opening and night ventilation it can be an indirect inﬂuence because the amount
of degree hours is inversely proportional with window-opening and night ventilation
where the amount of RH hours is proportional with window-opening and night venti-
lation. A lower air temperature in a room with the same moisture content also causes
higher RH and this can be an explanation for the high inﬂuence window-opening and
night ventilation have on RH hours. If a new building were to be built where it is known
that there is a high uncertainty of the moisture load and RH is an important factor the
ventilation control strategies with local sensors would be preferable. They are the strate-
gies which are most robust to a parameter variation of moisture load followed by VAV -
Central RH (p) which are 25% more sensitive to variation of the moisture load.
CO2 hours is for a large number of control strategies inﬂuenced the most by occu-
pancy load, window-opening and setpoint for space heating. Setpoint for space heating is
inversely proportional with CO2 hours since the window-opening is controlled by indoor
temperature and the setpoint for space heating determines how close the indoor tempera-
ture is to the setpoint for window-opening. Window-opening are neither proportional or
inversely proportional with CO2 hours but a combination of the two. The only possible
explanation for this is that window-opening sometimes interferes with the ventilation sys-
tem and lowers the ventilation rate. When the window-opens it lowers the temperature
and often also the moisture content, but only for the room with the window. If the venti-
87
==PAGE:112==
6. DISCUSSION
lation system is temperature or RH controlled it will detect lower levels of these control
parameters when the window is open and then lower the ventilation rate. Meanwhile CO2
concentration might be high in other rooms of the apartment where the window does not
open and because the ventilation system has lowered the ventilation rate the CO2 concen-
tration rises.
Design parameters that affect VAV - All (p), min
If it is desired to lower the primary energy consumption when using the ventilation control
strategy VAV - All (p), min, the most important thing to change is the setpoint for space
heating and window-opening. The sensitivity analysis also revealed glazing size to be
important, but because it is proportional with the primary energy consumption it is not
the extra solar heat gain that makes it important, it is the larger window-opening area.
This means it is actually window-opening that is important.
6.4
Uncertainty and sources of errors
Most simulation programs including the IDA ICE software are not able to simulate reality
with 100% accuracy and will be a source of error. Pavlovas compared results from IDA
ICE with real measurements on a Swedish residential building and found the results from
IDA ICE to match the real measurements to a high degree [14].
The doors were always closed in the models simulated and this does not match reality.
Closed doors restrict the movement of air and room concentrations of CO2, temperature
and RH will be higher in the simulated models than in reality. It is possible to have door
opening in the simulation models but the mathematical model is not accurate resulting in
a unrealistic high mixing in the apartment. The closed door with an opening area of 100
cm2 was the best approximation to the reality. A model with VAV - Central CO2 (p) is
simulated with and without door opening and the results can be seen in Table 6.1. Even
though the ventilation system is not running more the indoor climate is much better since
all the air is well mixed in the whole apartment. The best approximation has been made
but the source has a large inﬂuence on the indoor climate if it is not 100% correct.
In reality the control and measured data will also have delay and will be more impre-
cise which is not accounted for in IDA ICE. There are no uncertainties on sensors used
in IDA ICE and the control reach instantly opposite to the reality. The balanced airﬂows
where the supply airﬂow and return airﬂow are equal would be difﬁcult to obtain in reality
88
==PAGE:113==
6.4. Uncertainty and sources of errors

when a frequency converter and mechanical actuators are used.
In most residences there is often a manual override of components with boosted air-
ﬂows where the most common is the extractor hood in the kitchen. This was not imple-
mented in any of the models to simplify the models and avoid more manual entering of
values each time a design parameter was changed. An implementation of an extractor
hood would be a large advantage for central systems and especially systems without RH
sensor and partly temperature sensor.
Due to simulation time and the relatively easy setup, the Morris method was used to
make the sensitivity analysis, but there are other methods that are more accurate to deter-
mine the sensitivity of parameters.
The way the results are evaluated can hide some important knowledge. It is a balance
between overview, simpliﬁcation and how much the results can tell. All ways have their
strong and weak sides where the degree hours, CO2 hours and RH hours have the strong
side of creating a fast overview and are good to compare the results from different models.
To mention some weak sides RH hours is ﬁrst of all a weighted value based on ﬂoor area,
but could also be based on volume, surface area or exterior surface area. Other than that
the RH hours can also hide short periods with high levels of RH in the winter time which
is much more critical than if the RH is exceeding the limit a little but constantly over the
simulated year.
The range and distribution function of the parameters investigated are based on a lot
of literature, but some aspects had to be estimated due to lack of coverage in the literature.
Some of the literature used was old and might not reﬂect reality today. This will affect
the sensitivity analysis causing some design parameters to be more or less inﬂuential on
the evaluation parameters than in reality.
The simpliﬁcation made for the two design parameters moisture load and internal load
89
==PAGE:114==
6. DISCUSSION
was not adjusted to match the number of occupants (except for the shower load). Fixed
values were used which in reality would depend on the amount of occupants thus they are
sources of imprecision. This inﬂuences the impact these parameters have on the evalu-
ation parameters in the sensitivity analysis and their dependency on occupancy schedule
which most likely will be larger in reality.
The occupancy schedule was based on a Danish time use survey from 2008 and time
use might have changed since then. Occupancy schedule was simpliﬁed to use the same
occupancy schedule every day of the year. A more accurate approach would be to generate
more schedules e.g one for each weekday or ideally one per day of the entire year. The
amount of work required to implement this in the simulation models would be substantial,
because each occupancy schedule has to be manually typed into the simulation model.
Cooking did not occur in some of the generated occupancy schedules which was esti-
mated to be unrealistic when the schedule were used the entire simulation year. A cook-
ing activity was therefore inserted into the generated occupancy schedules and thus the
schedules was not entirely objectively generated. Another improvement to the occupancy
schedule could be to make a more advanced way and have memory of previous event
in order to adjust probabilities. This was done for some aspect like sleeping where one
sleeping activity could only occur once per day. This was based on estimations and not
empirical data from time use surveys.
These sources of errors in the occupancy schedule impact the evaluation of the venti-
lation control strategies, especially those sensitive to the occupancy schedule. They also
inﬂuence the ranking of design parameter impact.
Due to limitations in the IDA ICE software it was not possible to insert a coefﬁcient
of discharge (Cd) for night ventilation. Night ventilation had to use the same Cd value as
the window-opening in the daytime even though they have different opening angles. This
affected the impact of night ventilation in the sensitivity analysis.
When using opening angle as a parameter variation the opening area increases when
the window size increases, but this is not always the case in reality. When windows get to
a certain size they often have a fraction of the window that can be opened instead of the
entire window.
An optimisation was done for VAV - RH (p) in a typical model setup where the other
ventilation control strategies were based on earlier case studies made for the same apart-
ment [11] [37] [38]. If the time was available to optimise all the control strategies the
results for each ventilation control strategy could be different due to e.g other low and
90
==PAGE:115==
6.4. Uncertainty and sources of errors
high band for the controller. VAV - RH (p) performed better than the other control strate-
gies with central sensor, this might not be the case if it was not the only system to be
optimised.
The control of the ventilation heat recovery unit (VHR) was optimised in relation to
the VAV - RH (p) control strategy which can favours this strategy over the others. Ideally
an optimisation of the VHR control for each of the ten ventilation control strategy was
performed. Further on in the investigation a different VHR control then needs to be used
for each control strategy.
91
==PAGE:116==
==PAGE:117==
CHAPTER 7
Conclusion
The main objective was to investigate which control strategy performed best with regards
to primary energy consumption and indoor climate. Another objective was to investigate
which control strategy for mechanical ventilation in low-energy residences is the least
sensitive to parameter variations. The best performing control strategy for mechanical
ventilation is VAV - All (p), min. It is the least sensitive when varying the design parame-
ters used in the sensitivity analysis and it is capable of providing the best indoor climate
of all the ventilation control strategies investigated. The only control strategy able to pro-
vide the same indoor climate and robustness towards parameter variations is VAV - All
(p). It uses the same amount of sensors but has a minimum ventilation rate of 0.3 l/s
per m2 heated ﬂoor area instead of 0.1 l/s per m2 heated for area. The higher minimum
ventilation rate increases the primary energy consumption by 8% but unlike VAV - All (p),
min it complies with the current Danish building regulations that require a minimum ven-
tilation rate of 0.3 l/s per m2 heated ﬂoor area. VAV - All (p), min has the second highest
primary energy consumption of all the investigated control strategies and VAV - All (p)
has the highest and that is because energy is required to provide a good indoor climate
by ventilation. When simulated with typical design parameters VAV - All (p) has a pri-
mary energy consumption of 19 kWh/m2/year1 and the limit for BC20 is 20 kWh/m2/year
which also includes energy for domestic hot water. Hence it is important to be aware of
other building parameters when using VAV - All (p) to lower the primary energy consump-
tion, otherwise it can be a challenge to comply with the energy requirements for Building
Class 2020. VAV - RH (p) also performed well and used 32%2 less primary energy than
VAV - All (p), but it also has a higher amount of degree hours and CO2 hours.
The hypothesis was that an advanced ventilation system in residential buildings with
local humidity, CO2 and temperature sensors can provide better indoor climate at a lower
1Excluding domestic hot water
2For typical design parameters
93
==PAGE:118==
7. CONCLUSION
energy consumption compared to less advanced ventilation systems. The best performing
control strategies VAV - All (p), min and VAV - All (p) is also the most advanced control
strategies. The ventilation control strategy with CAV performed well despite its simple
control. It uses 16%2 less primary energy than VAV - All (p) and has 11% more degree
hours and 90% fewer CO2 hours than VAV - RH (p). The strategy using CAV is more
sensitive on primary energy consumption and RH hours but far more robust on CO2 hours
and also more robust on degree hours than VAV - RH (p).
Another objective was to investigate which building related parameter has the largest
inﬂuence on the performance of ventilation systems in low-energy residences. The sen-
sitivity analysis revealed that window-opening is a very important design parameter that
inﬂuences the primary energy consumption and the three evaluation parameters for all
the ventilation control strategies investigated. Setpoint for space heating is very inﬂuen-
tial on the primary energy consumption e.g. a level change in the model with both CAV
or VAV - All (p), min results in an average change of approximately 4.2 kWh/m2/year in
the primary energy consumption.
The study also found that the ventilation control strategies using a central sensor in the
exhaust duct to measure the exhaust air have a poor performance compared to the other
investigated control strategies. The reason why these control strategies have a bad perfor-
mance is because a room in the apartment could have a very bad indoor climate without
the control strategies being capable of detecting it. This is because the air measured by
the sensor in the exhaust duct is a mixing of air from the rooms with the exhaust units.
94
==PAGE:119==
CHAPTER 8
Further work
The investigation is based on one apartment with four rooms in Copenhagen which is not
representative for the Danish building mass. To make the study more representative for
the Danish building mass several other residences need to be investigated and a similar
sensitivity analysis need to be performed for these as well.
The use of a reduced ventilation airﬂow need to be investigated more thorough to docu-
ment that no residential building type will have issues with bad indoor environment due
to the reduced ventilation air ﬂow.
The analysis can be done with the design parameter occupancy load and occupancy sched-
ule improved and where they are made more realistic and variate over the year. Other
design parameters as moisture load and internal load can be optimised by adjusting the
values to match the amount of occupants.
The sensitivity analysis can also be extended to cover more ventilation control strategies
e.g optimised versions of the control strategies used in this study with a central sensor.
The optimisation of ventilation control strategies can also be based on a sensitivity anal-
ysis so each strategy is optimised to be as robust as possible and at the same time deliver
a good indoor climate while having a low energy consumption.

bined living room and kitchen.

FIFTH APPENDIX

144
==PAGE:169==
Sixth appendix
A description of the calculation sheet for the raw calculation of which activity is happen-
ing when and how long time it happens. In total it is made for 7 persons over a period
of 24 hours. The calculation is done 7 individual times creating the 7 possible schedules
which afterwards are combined into the apartment with a randomly number of persons/
schedules. The description only covers one of the calculations.
The ﬁrst columns from A to K in the sheet is constant numbers of the cumulative prob-
ability for each of the 10 activities to happen in that time step. There is a new probability
in each time step of 10 minutes during a day resulting in 144 rows. The last columns from
AJ to AT in the sheet is constant numbers of the cumulative probability of each activity
happening for a duration periods with an interval of 10 minutes. For each interval there
is a probability that the activity is occurring in that many minutes or less. Since there is
a probability that you sleep or is bedridden for nearly a whole day there is 143 rows for
each of the 10 activities with the cumulative probability for this.
The randomly selection of what activity is going on are performed in column M to
column Y. It is based on the cumulative probability for the activity happening at that time
and the cumulative probability for the duration period. A selection is made for every
interval for all ten activities resulting in 1440 individual cells with a script randomly
selecting what is going to happen for this activity in this interval. In column M a random
number between 0-1 is selected which is transformed in column N to a selection of activity
based on the cumulative probability for the activity happening in that interval. In column
O a random number is generated to use when selecting the duration time of the happening
which is done individually in each of the 1440 cells.
The starting point is deﬁned at 20:00 because 00:00 did not function as a starting point
since it would divide the sleeping activity into two. 20:00 is a good break point before
people really begin to sleep and after they have returned to their homes. The script is
different for four types of activity where for these four activity it is only possible that a
145
==PAGE:170==
SIXTH APPENDIX
long period of the activity is happening once per day. This is implemented to avoid the
whole day is spend on this activity and therefore a duration period of 30 minutes is used
after the activity already has happen once that day. The four types of activity are sleeping,
family care, relaxing and not home since the probability for those activity happening more
than 5 hours in a row are above 10%. This is called the script with memory.
Below is the script for a single cell shown for both the cases where memory is used
and where it is not used. The script without memory is for the toilette activity is happening
from 00:00-00:10 and the script with memory is for the sleeping activity happening from
00:00-00:10.
The standard script without memory:
=HVISER($Q147>10,1,$P147>10,0,$R147>10,0,$S147>10,0,$T147>10,0,$U147>10,0
,$V147>10,0,$W147>10,0,$X147>10,0,$Y147>10,0,
$N4=2,1,$N4>2.5,0,$N4<1.5,0)*
VIS(Q147>10,Q147-10,
(HVISER($O4<AL$4,$AJ$3,...,$O4<AL$73,$AJ$72,$O4>AL$73,0)+HVISER($O4<
AL$73,0,$O4<AL$74,$AJ$73,...,O4<AL$147,$AJ$146)))
Blue script = If the activity is already happening then it gives 1 and if there is other ac-
tivity happening then it gives 0, Green script = If there is no activity happening then it
gives 1 if the happening is selected in column N, Orange script = If a activity is already
happening 10 minutes are subtracted otherwise the red script, Red script = Is deﬁning
how long time span the activity should happen.
The script whit memory:
=HVISER($P147>10,1,$Q147>10,0,$R147>10,0,$S147>10,0,$T147>10,0,$U147>10,0,$V147>10,0,
$W147>10,0,$X147>10,0,$Y147>10,0,
$N4=1,1,$N4>1.5,0)*
HVIS(P147>10,P147-10,
HVIS(P124+P125+...+P147>0,30,
(HVISER($O4<AK$4,$AJ$3,...,$O4<AK$73,$AJ$72,$O4>AK$73,0)+HVISER($O4<AK$73,0,$O4<
AK$74,$AJ$73,...,$O4<AK$147,$AJ$146))))
Blue script = If the activity is already happening then it gives 1 and if there is other activ-
ity happening then it gives 0, Green script = If there is no activity happening then it gives
1 if the happening is randomly selected in column N, Orange script = If the activity is
already happening 10 minutes is subtracted, Yellow script = Otherwise 30 minutes is set
if the activity already has happen one time that day and if not the red script, Red script =
Is deﬁning how long time span the activity should happen.
146
==PAGE:171==
Seventh appendix
The calculation of how many hours the AHU used in the simulations models cannot meet
the RH requirements is based on the same equation 4.3 used for the dimension of the
AHU in the case with moisture production. In Table 1 an example for the ﬁrst 24 hours
of the year is inserted where the RH is found for each hour. The calculation is based
on maximum supply of 100.6 l/s, typical design parameters and DRY2013 weather ﬁle
combined with the assumption of full mixing in the apartment and a indoor temperature
limit of up to 27◦C can be fully utilised in order to minimise the RH hours. Hours where
the RH is calculated to be above 60% is summarised to be 40 hours over a year and if the
the RH hours is calculated it responds to 147 RH hours.
147
